Large Stock Focus: GM, Morgan Stanley Help Depress Stocks --- Jobless Data Fuel Fears of Recession; Retailers Hit Hard
Large Stock Focus: GM, Morgan Stanley Help Depress Stocks --- Jobless Data Fuel Fears of
Recession; Retailers Hit Hard
By Rob Curran
846 words
10 October 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5A
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks fell sharply again Thursday as selling of car makers such as General Motors and financial stocks
such as Morgan Stanleyfed on itself.
Fears that a prolonged recession is on its way were heightened by a report that the latest weekly continuing
jobless claims were at their highest since June 2003. Consumer-discretionary stocks, or those retailers that
sell nonessential items, were particularly hard hit.
"People are just acting on fear," said Debra Brede, president of DK Brede Investment Management. "Their
grandfathers are going 'I went through the Great Depression,' and the mentality right now is we're all going to
lose everything."
General Motors was the leading decliner on the Dow by a long shot, falling 2.15, or 31%, to 4.76, its lowest
level since at least 1950, according to the Center for Research in Securities Prices at the University of
Chicago.
Late in the session, Standard &Poor's Ratings Services placed its ratings for General Motors, Ford Motor and
their finance arms on watch for further downgrade, citing the rapidly weakening global automotive market and
worsening capital-market conditions.
In recent months, GM has addressed the fears that it can't survive the economic crisis.
General Motors is focused on executing the $15 billion liquidity-boosting plan first announced in July, a
spokeswoman said Thursday.
Shares of rival Ford Motor fell 58 cents, or 22%, to 2.08.
Office Depot fell 85 cents, or 23%, to 2.82. Abercrombie &Fitch slid 4.82, or 15%, to 27.69.
Banks, lenders and insurance stocks crashed in the wake of the expiry of the Securities and Exchange
Commission's short-selling ban, and as repeated efforts by central banks and governments to break the
freeze in short-term lending markets made little headway. The Financial Select Sector SPDR, an
exchange-traded fund that traders use as a proxy for the sector, slid 1.59, or 10%, to 13.69. The S&P 500
financial sector fell 15% on this session alone.
Morgan Stanley dropped 4.35, or 26%, to 12.45, leading the financial sector lower despite reports the U.S.
government may help recapitalize banks. Mitsubishi UFJ Financial reiterated its commitment to inject capital
in Morgan Stanley, denying rumors to the contrary.
On the options market, about 46,000 contracts carrying the right to sell Morgan for $5 in October changed
hands.
Among other financial stocks emerging from the cover of the short-sale ban, insurance companies were
particularly hard hit.
XL Capital plunged 4.67, or 54%, to 4.01; Hartford Financial Services Group fell 4.75, or 19%, to 20.11; and
Prudential Financial lost 10.02, or 23%, to 33.27.
Recently, Hartford and peer MetLife have warned of losses in their investment portfolios.
Plus, the insurers may face demands for collateral in a business intended as a sideline, called securities
lending.

--- Page 34 ---
Page 34 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In securities lending, insurers "loan" fixed-income securities from their investment portfolios to banks or other
so-called borrowers, who then post collateral, usually in the form of cash or Treasury securities. With much of
the investment community facing dire straits, the insurers may be forced to return the cash.
One institution in that business, American International Group, fell 80 cents, or 25%, to 2.39. AIG had to
borrow another $37.8 billion from the federal government, as an earlier $85 billion loan to support the
company was apparently insufficient.
Fear indicators, such as the Chicago Board Options Exchange volatility index, hit all-time highs. The VIX is
known as the market's "fear gauge" because traders pay up for protection when nerves are jangled.
The Dow Jones Wilshire 5000 index, which includes almost all U.S. companies, has peeled off $8.4 trillion in
wealth since the all-time high it hit a year ago Thursday.
"The snowball of fear that has set off an avalanche of collapsing financial institutions has created an
altogether new regime for volatility," said Andrew Wilkinson, a strategist at Interactive Brokers, in a note to
clients.
Suppliers of those products that people turn to in the worst of times fared somewhat better.
Drug store CVS Caremark rose 54 cents, or 1.8%, to 30.38.
Beer brewer Anheuser-Busch was off 1.31, or 2.1%, to 61.61.
SPDR Gold Shares tacked on 48 cents, or 0.5%, to 89.90.
Huntsman rose 2.56, or 28%, to 11.64 after leveraged-buyout firm Apollo Management agreed to give its
specialty chemicals unit $540 million to help it close a $6.5 billion purchase of the chemicals maker.
While traders warned that the current market environment is treacherous and reminiscent of the most brutal
of markets past, some are still braving the waters: Ms. Brede of DK Brede Investment Management, who
manages $800 million in assets, said she bought shares of a prominent financial stock this week.

Credit Hurts Commodities
Credit Hurts Commodities
By Ann Davis and Carolyn Cui
979 words
10 October 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5B
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The credit crisis is contributing to the decline of commodities markets as investors grow increasingly worried
about doing business with the financial firms that create complex products pegged to oil, gold and grain
markets.
Interest in these products has boomed in recent years, but defaults and some surprising losses on
supposedly safe investments could sour investors on the sector.
Many investors, such as pension or mutual funds, don't buy commodity futures directly but instead purchase
from banks and brokerages "structured notes" and other instruments that provide a return linked to a
commodity benchmark.
Amid the credit crisis, holders of these Wall Street products are growing worried that what they own isn't
crude oil or pork bellies, but a mere promise by a bank to pay. As they watched financial institutions scramble
to secure funding lifelines, several have sold investments made with once-strong commodity-market dealers
whom they now deem to be risky counterparties.
The third quarter marked the first time since 2003 that commodity assets under management fell, mainly
because commodity prices plummeted about 30%. Barclays Capital, a unit of Barclays PLC, estimates
institutional investors also took out $5 billion from investments tracking commodity indexes in the third
quarter, in addition to watching their holdings of $175 billion fall to $120 billion.
Barclays says issuance of bond-like exchange-traded notes have plateaued after a torrid business in the first
half of 2008. Financial institutions raised $22 billion this year on such commodity-related notes, double that of
the same period in 2007, according to mtn-i, a London data provider.
Commodity mutual-fund holders also are learning that some managers parked cash in seemingly safe
fixed-income securities as collateral that lately has been anything but safe. Hits to this collateral, intended to
provide liquidity for withdrawals and to boost returns, have caused some widely held funds to significantly
underperform the indexes they are designed to track. Among those facing losses are an OppenheimerFunds
vehicle that helped pioneer natural-resources investing, as well as a fund run by PIMCO, which counts some
large pension funds as its clients.
Commodity prices also are falling as fears grow that U.S. and European economic woes are sparking a global
contagion that could crush raw-materials demand.
Investors spooked by counterparty credit risks are adding to the pressure by backing out of the notes or
over-the-counter derivatives called swaps. One small BlackRock Inc. commodities fund is liquidating all its
assets. Hedge funds are dumping better-performing commodity bets just to reduce leverage.
It is too early to tell whether investors' love affair with commodities will reverse. Many analysts and financial
advisers remain bullish about the long-term prospects for energy and other raw materials, as well as the
diversification benefits that they say commodities provide.
Analysts at Barclays Capital said this week that investors are revising their strategies but commodities "do not
appear to be suffering a higher level of withdrawals than any other asset class."
Nonetheless, trust is rapidly eroding in brokerage firms that had enjoyed a highly lucrative business
structuring commodity investments.
"We certainly have seen people reallocating their exposure to a higher-credit counterparty. There's been a
move away from broker-dealers to banks," says Philippe J.J. Comer, head of commodity investor solutions for
the Americas at Barclays. As a unit of a commercial bank, Barclays Capital is the recipient of some
reallocated money.

--- Page 36 ---
Page 36 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The credit crisis also is boosting the trading of traditional futures contracts, some money managers say.
Payments are guaranteed through exchange clearinghouses, and therefore offer more security than privately
negotiated derivatives. This might shrink the amount of commodities under management, because regulatory
restrictions prevent financial speculators from making futures trades beyond a certain size.
Credit anxiety rose abruptly in mid-September when Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc. filed for bankruptcy
protection and the financial conglomerate American International Group Inc., a big commodities dealer,
narrowly avoided the same fate with a federal rescue deal that left the direction of its commodities desk in
limbo.
Lehman's commodities desk walked away from its obligations, prompting lawsuits from counterparties
including energy investor T. Boone Pickens and Bank of America. AIG says it is honoring its commitments.
Criterion Investment Ltd., a Toronto-based hedge fund, recently redeemed all the commodity-linked notes
issued by AIG in a $25.3 million commodities fund, citing "uncertainty regarding the note issuer."
BlackRock said in a September filing that it will liquidate its $32 million Commodity Strategies Fund by the
end of October. At the end of May, it held 18.5% of the fund's assets in a structured note issued by AIG-FP
Private Funding Corp. It also held some assets in mortgage-backed securities.
BlackRock attributed the four-year-old fund's closure to "current market conditions" and the difficulty, given its
small size, of spreading counterparty risk among multiple note issuers.
Structured notes, including exchange-traded, are like any bond backed by financial institutions, except that
they guarantee the principal plus the return of a basket of commodities or, in some cases, make payments
pegged to both commodities and interest rates. Buyers take on the credit risk of the issuer in case of default.
Many commodity mutual funds invest in structured notes rather than other derivatives because of tax rules.
With swaps, another popular instrument, counterparty risk is a concern but not as great, because the client
doesn't hand over all its principal. Instead, the swap holder settles up with a bank on a regular basis by
swapping payments tied to the return of different instruments. Sometimes the bank owes the client;
sometimes the client owes the bank.
Commodity investments have met other big snags involving the collateral set aside to post margin on futures
accounts, meet investor redemptions, or boost returns.

MarketBeat / Market Insight from WSJ.com
MarketBeat / Market Insight from WSJ.com
By David Gaffen
436 words
9 October 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Yet Another Worrisome Signal
---
Gold-to-Oil Ratio May Augur More Dollar Doldrums
If Crude Continues to Fall and Yellow Metal Outperforms
Through most of 2006, 2007 and early 2008, commodities were ascendant, led by crude oil and gold. Oil
started to run ahead of gold in mid-2008, but the roles have reversed. What the movement in this ratio
signifies is another potential reason for worry.
One ratio analysts look at is the ratio of gold prices to oil prices to determine where the economy might be
headed. Often, this ratio is steady in strong economic times. When oil begins to do better, outperforming gold,
the ratio falls, emblematic of strong growth, but also supply shocks or inflation concerns.
At some point, this ratio ends up hitting a bottom. Then, gold starts to outperform, and the ratio increases,
and with it, a signal that a recession is in progress or on the way. Over the past 37 years, this ratio has had a
monthly average of 13; that is, gold's price in dollars is 13 times the price of oil, said Ashraf Laidi, strategist at
CMC Markets.
"The ratio elucidates the relative performance of these two essential commodities whereby a rise or rebound
in the ratio is usually synonymous with deteriorating global economic conditions," Mr. Laidi said. "Oil goes
down when things are bad, and gold goes up when economically things are bad."
The gold-to-oil ratio bottomed out in July at 5.8, when oil prices peaked at about $145 a barrel. Since then,
the ratio has increased, even as gold and oil have both fluctuated. Oil settled lower Wednesday at $88.95 a
barrel, while gold finished up at $903.10 a troy ounce, pushing the ratio up to 10.15. Mr. Laidi notes that the
rebound in the gold-oil ratio was accompanied by a recession, a decline in the dollar's strength, and more
interest-rate cuts from the Federal Reserve.
Oil's decline has been sharp, in part a result of the unwinding of bets made by hedge funds and other
investors in the commodities market.
Tom Bentz, senior energy analyst at BNP Paribas Commodity Futures, said oil may be in line for more
weakness ahead. "If the global economy continues to unravel, we can get back below $86 and, perhaps,
down near $78," he said.
If that is the case, and gold continues to outperform, it shows that the dollar may sustain more damage, and
that the Fed's half-point cut Wednesday may not be its last.

Investors Rush Back to Gold --- Crude Oil Declines In Volatile Session; Woes Hit Copper
Commodities Report
Investors Rush Back to Gold --- Crude Oil Declines In Volatile Session; Woes Hit Copper
By Gregory Meyer
622 words
9 October 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C9
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold futures rose on Wednesday, reasserting their status as a safe-haven investment, while crude oil fell
during a session made volatile by a coordinated cut of interest rates around the world.
The half-point rate cut, which included action by central banks in the U.K., Canada, Sweden and Switzerland,
was seen as a confidence booster for faltering financial markets. It followed a series of drastic measures by
many governments to shore up the global banking sector. But the move also cast a pall as it underscored
how bad market conditions have become.
Light, sweet crude for November delivery hit a 10-month intraday low before springing into positive territory
following the rate-cut announcement. Trading was choppy throughout the day, and the contract settled $1.11,
or 1.2%, lower at $88.95 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Contracts for later months ended
slightly higher on the day.
Nearby October gold on the Comex division of the Nymex settled 3.1% higher at $903.10 a troy ounce after
rising as high as $924.90 in the wake of the announcement.
The rate cuts effectively acted as a reminder of the financial crisis and its reverberations throughout the
economy. While investors turn to gold in times of crisis because it tends to retain value, real demand for
other commodities, such as oil, correlates with economic growth.
"People are looking at these measures as a stabilization move," said Lawrence Eagles, global head of
commodities research at J.P. Morgan Chase &Co. "But they are looking beyond the financial crisis, and
revising down their global [gross domestic product] projections for next year. That obviously has implications
for oil demand growth."
U.S. oil demand in the past four weeks was 8.6% lower than a year earlier, according to U.S. data. Last week
domestic oil use was the lightest since September 2001.
Energy traders spent much of this year shrugging off statistics showing falling U.S. oil demand in the face of
oil prices that skyrocketed north of $145 a barrel. Those with bullish bets had faith that emerging economies,
such as China, would make up for the shortfall. That belief is under siege. The People's Bank of China joined
Wednesday's coordinated action, although it cut rates by less than half a percentage point.
Gold, however, remains a popular bet. Strong demand has led to the U.S. Mint to broaden its freeze on gold
bullion coins. Daniel Pavilonis, a senior market strategist with Lind-Waldock, a futures brokerage, said a lot of
"everyday investors" are getting into gold.
"You're seeing a lot of physical buying because of the turmoil in the stock market," Pavilonis said. "Maybe it's
turning to a demand and lack-of-supply issue."
In other commodity markets:
COPPER: Prices hit their lowest level in 1 1/2 years on worries about the economy and demand, especially
with the stock market on the defensive for much of a volatile session. Industrial commodities such as copper
are weak at a time of worries about a potential recession, says Scott Meyers, senior trading analyst with
Pioneer Futures. December copper on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange lost 17.95
cents to $2.3550 a pound.
SOYBEANS: Futures on the Chicago Board of Trade climbed as traders bought back previously sold
positions. U.S. harvest delays because of rainy Midwestern weather and little farmer selling also underpinned
prices as traders turned away from looking at the global credit crunch and toward soybean-specific news.
November soybeans gained 38 cents to $9.64 a bushel.

Sharp Declines In Commodities Hammer Funds --- Fears of Global Downturn Fuel Reversal After Big Run-Up; Gold Goes Its Own Way
Sharp Declines In Commodities Hammer Funds --- Fears of Global Downturn Fuel Reversal After Big
Run-Up; Gold Goes Its Own Way
By Shefali Anand
1,361 words
9 October 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Until earlier this year, commodity-focused mutual funds were red hot. Now, they're burning through the value
of investors' portfolios.
Over the past three months through Tuesday, these funds and other commodity-linked investments such as
exchange-traded notes, are down on average 26% to 44%, according to Lipper Inc., thanks to a broad selloff
in several commodities since mid-July. This is a sharp reversal from the big gains in these funds in the past
three years. In 2007, for instance, average gains for these funds ranged from 23% to 40%.
The commodity slide is broad. Crude oil is down nearly 35% over the past three months, while metals like
silver and platinum are down 34% and 49%, respectively. One commodity that has held its own is gold as
investors have flocked to the precious metal as a haven amid recent turmoil in the markets. The metal is
down only 2% in the past three months.
Commodity prices have come down as more investors have begun to feel that a global economic slowdown
will likely reduce demand for commodities and metals. Also, in recent weeks, some large commodity funds
and hedge funds have faced investor redemptions or have had to shut down and sell their commodity
positions, thus depressing the price of various commodities and commodity stocks.
"There's essentially this global deleveraging, where every asset is being sold," says Mihir Worah, manager of
the Pimco CommodityRealReturn fund.
On average, mutual funds and exchange-traded funds that invest in oil, gas and other natural-resources
companies are down the most, with an average 44% decline in the past three months. Funds that invest in
companies related to precious metals such as gold and silver have fallen on average 37% over three months
and 35% since the start of the year.
Mutual funds and ETFs that invest in the stocks of energy and metals companies are hurting more than the
funds that invest in the underlying commodities directly or through derivative contracts. For instance, the U.S.
Global Investors World Precious Minerals fund, which has half its investments in gold-mining companies like
Goldcorp Inc., has lost far more than the SPDR Gold Shares ETF, which owns gold bullion. Mining and other
commodity companies have been hit hard because investors now believe the companies' profits will be
depressed for some time. Also, funds that are more narrowly focused have taken a larger hit. The Market
Vectors Coal ETF, which invests only in coal-oriented companies, is down 60% for the past three months,
according to Lipper.
Commodity-oriented funds were popular with investors in the past few years because the funds paid heady
gains and served as a hedge against inflation. Fund companies fueled investors' appetite by launching a
slew of products: Around 70 of the 154 such mutual funds, ETFs and exchange-traded notes tracked by
Lipper were launched within the past two years. These funds hold a total of nearly $119 billion in assets.
Sharp Reversal
But the recent sharp reversal to steep losses from double-digit gains within months underscores the volatility
inherent in many of these investments.
Tom Drake, a 70-year-old retired medical doctor from Phoenix, is among those who have already pulled out.
At the beginning of this year, he had 30% of his portfolio in four commodity funds, including a metals fund, an
energy stock fund and two funds that bought various commodities. He had bought most of these funds in the
past two years, as a hedge against inflation. But in mid-July, as prices fell sharply, he sold out of the bulk of
them.
Commodities "tend to go up very strongly and then they drop down to a plateau and stay there for six to eight
months," he says. This time he thinks the plateau may last 18 months. "I think this is a big correction in the

--- Page 44 ---
Page 44 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
middle of long bull market in commodities and gold." In the past few days, he has boosted his investment in a
gold ETF, but commodities are now 15% of his portfolio. Reassessing Companies
Funds that invest in stocks of small and medium-sized energy and mining companies have been among the
worst hit lately. As commodity prices have fallen, investors are reassessing their outlook for these companies.
Among the funds affected are U.S. Global Investors' Global Resources and World Precious Minerals funds,
which have the bulk of their assets in medium-sized companies and are down 54% and 49%, respectively, for
three months.
"There's been more delays and disappointments in the [production from] midcaps and small caps," says
Frank Holmes, manager of the funds. Over the past five years, these funds are up 16% and 10% annualized,
respectively.
Funds that invest in derivatives like commodity-linked structured notes, which are debt instruments that
provide exposure to commodities without requiring investors to own them, haven't declined as much as those
that invest in the stocks of commodity companies. Funds investing in commodity derivatives are down an
average 26%. On the flip side, they didn't run up as much in previous months.
One reason for this disparity is that stock funds, often called "natural resources" or "gold and precious
metals" funds, are affected by the general nervousness surrounding the stock market.
"Investors just want to be in cash and they're selling every asset class," says Mr. Worah of the Pimco
CommodityRealReturn fund.
His $12.4 billion fund, one of the largest commodity mutual funds, buys derivative instruments to provide
exposure to a broad range of commodities like oil, natural gas, precious metals, livestock and grains. It also
invests in Treasury Inflation Protected Securities, which are a hedge against inflation. This diversification
helped the fund until a month ago, but it has been hit by the recent selloff. The fund is down 39% over the
past three months, but up 8.6% annualized over the past five years through Tuesday, according to Lipper.
To navigate the current market, Mr. Holmes of U.S. Global Investors has invested small amounts in an
inverse fund (which promises to return the inverse of a market index) to manage the downside, and has
increased his funds' cash position to around 20% to meet redemption requests from investors. He says the
funds have seen only modest redemptions so far. Currency Hit
Steve Land, manager of the Franklin Gold and Precious Metals, notes that funds like his also are hurting
from the recent run-up of the U.S. dollar against some foreign currencies. Many gold-mining companies are
based abroad, and when foreign stock returns are calculated back in the U.S. dollar, they appear weaker if
the dollar has risen. The Franklin fund, which has a bulk of its investment in gold company stocks, is down
nearly 43.6% for the past three months but is up 9% annualized over the past five years.
Investors in ETFs that hold physical gold bullion have held up the best. The $19.3 billion SPDR Gold Shares
or the $1.8 billion iShares Comex Gold ETF are down only around 4.5% and 5.4%, respectively, for the past
three months.
Given the wild swings that come with being a commodity investor, advisers typically suggest only small
investments in commodity-oriented investments. Tom Orecchio, a fee-only planner in Old Tappan, N.J., puts
around 6% to 7% of his clients' portfolios in broad-based commodity funds. Now that commodities have gone
down, he's buying to bring his allocations back to the 6% to 7% target. "We like commodities because they're
good diversifiers," Mr. Orecchio said.

thanks to a broad selloff in commodities.

funds, but funds that invest in mining stocks were hit hard.

Shipping Lines Sail Uncertain Seas --- Global Weakness Sends Rates Tumbling With New Vessels on the Way; Some May Become Cruise Ships
Shipping Lines Sail Uncertain Seas --- Global Weakness Sends Rates Tumbling With New Vessels on
the Way; Some May Become Cruise Ships
By John W. Miller
770 words
8 October 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Brussels -- Last year, the basic price of shipping a large container of goods from Asia to Europe, the world's
busiest route, was $2,800. This week, with demand plunging amid a worsening economy, that price was an
unprofitable $700.
That rate is "unsustainable," says Eivind Kolding, chief executive of Copenhagen-based A.P. Moller Maersk
AS, the world's biggest shipping company by sales. The industry would be crippled if that price doesn't rise
soon, he says.
Hit by the global economic downturn and a financial meltdown that promises an even sharper drop in
once-hot trade flows, container-shipping companies are cutting routes and capacity to stem a sudden flow of
red ink.
Making their plight worse, the container shippers during boom times ordered fleets of new vessels for as
much as $50 million apiece that are getting delivered only now -- just as business dries up. Analysts say
companies likely will try to cancel their orders, sell the ships, or even convert them to tankers or cruise
vessels.
The plight of the $150 billion container-shipping industry shows how the credit crunch and global downturn
have caught up to companies that make investment decisions years in advance.
Other shipping routes, including Asia-U.S. lanes, also are suffering from declining demand. But the U.S. has
tighter harbor space than Europe. Prices for the smaller ships docking in California, Texas and the East
Coast have settled around a barely profitable $1,500 a container, analysts say.
The past 10 years were a gold rush for shippers. China joined the World Trade Organization and sold
hundreds of billions of dollar of goods to European and American consumers, who were enjoying low interest
rates and steady economic growth. Factories relocated to Asia, stretching supply chains around the globe.
Oil was cheap, ships were relatively scarce and shipping prices soared.
Container-shipping traffic on the Asia-Europe route rose at roughly 15% a year through the period. This year
it will increase just 5%, says Philip Damas, of London-based maritime consultant Drewry Shipping
Consultants Ltd. Capacity is growing much faster. "There's a glut of new large container ships entering the
market," Mr. Damas says.
Freight-shipping prices are notoriously volatile, depending on weather, ever-changing capacity and shifting
trade flows. But the recent slide is unprecedented, analysts and shipping-company executives say.
Maersk, the world's No. 1 shipper with sales of $51.2 billion last year, waited until Thursday to cut container
rates to their present low. Mr. Kolding, the Maersk CEO, told trade publication Lloyd's List that he hadn't seen
the collapse coming. He promised "changes" later this month. That means running fewer vessels in a bid to
run up prices, and maybe dropping an Asia-Europe route, a Maersk official said.
Rates for ships heading from Europe to Asia are even more depressed than the reverse trip -- about $200 per
40-foot container. These days, 60% of containers making that trip are empty, reflecting Europe's trade deficit
with China, which stood at $223 billion last year.
Zim Integrated Shipping Services Ltd. on Saturday canceled its new Asia-Europe route, which it started in
January with nine ships. Zim, based in Haifa, Israel, says it is betting that rates will pick up in time for the
arrival late next year of two mammoth container ships being built.
Eager to cash in on the trade boom, companies such as Zim a few years ago put in a wave of orders to South
Korean and Chinese shipyards. Those boats are being launched now. Marseille-based CMA CGM SA, for

--- Page 46 ---
Page 46 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
example, has placed orders for 80 ships, at a total cost of roughly $1 billion, to complement the some 400 it
already runs. Geneva-based Mediterranean Shipping Company SA is scheduled to receive 57 ships, adding
to its 427.
Maersk received 15 vessels in the first half and has 48 ships on order for delivery by 2012. "Given the present
market conditions we're quite pleased that we don't have the largest order book in the industry," says
spokesman Michael Christian Storgaard.
Maersk officials decline to disclose their plans for the new ships, but, if demand collapses, there are few
options, says Dirk Visser, an analyst with Dynamar BV, a consulting firm based in Alkmaar, the Netherlands.
"The best option is to convert the ship to a tanker or a cruise ship, then sell it at a loss," he says.

We Won't Suffer a Japanese Deflation
We Won't Suffer a Japanese Deflation
By David Gitlitz
873 words
8 October 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A17
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
As U.S. credit markets continue to be roiled in chaos, some are bandying about the notion that America's
problems resemble those of Japan in its deflationary "lost decade" of the 1990s. "Deflation looms. It certainly
does loom," said one functionary for a major international bank. "The cycle in which debt destruction and
asset price destruction reinforce each other clearly has a very, very, strong negative effect on the economy."
This analysis expresses a common fallacy that asset-price declines give rise to economic weakness, and the
effect is therefore deflationary. But "deflation" is not a synonym for economic contraction. Deflation is rather a
sustained decline in the overall price level, i.e., the opposite of inflation. Like inflation, deflation is a
monetary phenomenon.
There is no evidence that deflationary influences are now at work in the U.S. economy. I was very familiar
with the Japanese deflation, having been the first to recognize and name it in a 1995 op-ed on this page. I
was exposed to considerable public criticism by the Bank of Japan at the time, but history has shown my
diagnosis to be entirely correct.
Aside from some superficial similarities, the current U.S. financial market disturbance bears no resemblance
to the economic misery that afflicted Japan for more than a decade, and in important ways continues to linger
there. In fact, the comparison should provide some comfort to Americans. U.S. monetary conditions are
nearly the exact opposite of the devastating deflation that characterized the Japanese experience.
The U.S. had its real-estate bubble through the first six years or so of the current decade, and on the surface,
that might seem comparable to the real-estate bubble that preceded Japan's decade of deflation. Our bubble
had its roots in the Fed's exceptionally accommodative monetary policy -- a situation not unlike Japan
through the late 1980s, when the Bank of Japan was also too easy for too long. But unlike the Fed, the BoJ
turned toward tightness with a vengeance, apparently with the objective -- at least initially -- of pricking the
bubble.
Japanese land prices began their long fall in 1991 on the heels of a sharp currency appreciation in 1990, with
the yen soaring nearly 20% against both the dollar and gold. That was just the beginning. By 1995, the
yen/dollar would see a nearly 50% appreciation, and the BoJ's deflationary bias remained in place for a
number of years. The relentless rise in the currency's purchasing power magnified the real burden of yen
debt, crushing borrowers and crippling the Japanese banking system.
Contrast that with the U.S. experience, in which the decline in real-estate values would coincide not with a
deflationary appreciation of the dollar, but an inflationary depreciation. From the time home prices peaked in
mid-2006 through the currency's lows last spring, the trade-weighted value of the dollar fell by some 18%.
Over the same period, the price of gold rose by about 75%. While the dollar has rebounded moderately in the
last several months, by any objective measure it remains in a weak position. On a trade-weighted basis, it
has returned to its levels of about a year ago. But before doing so, it had never been weaker. At around $830
in gold terms, the dollar has recovered a modicum of the purchasing power lost when gold soared above
$1,000 last March in the midst of the Bear Stearns calamity. But at current levels the price of gold is double
what it was four years ago.
The relative damage to real-estate values between the U.S. and Japan is instructive. Thus far, U.S. home
prices have fallen about 12.5% from their peak. But they remain about 40% above their 2000 levels. In Japan,
by 2001 the destruction of values brought land prices down to about half their levels of the late 1980s.
The U.S. housing downturn and associated financial-market turbulence is attributable not to tight monetary
conditions, but to an unsustainable speculative bubble triggered by loose monetary conditions. The current
market turmoil might well put the economy into at least a shallow and short-lived recession. But unlike Japan,
the U.S. economy will not have to dig its way out of a debilitating, long-lasting monetary deflation.

--- Page 48 ---
Page 48 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
On the contrary, the current economic climate is marked by a considerable upswing in inflation, with the
headline consumer price index now running at about 5.4%, up from less than 2% a year ago. The decline in
crude oil prices will keep down the reported rate for a few months. But once oil stops falling, the underlying
inflationary influences will reassert themselves, and no sizeable long-lasting decline in reported inflation is
likely in the foreseeable future.
The "lost decade" of stagnation and monetary deflation, and its remaining legacy today, were the product of a
persistently too-tight Bank of Japan. The Fed was not tight even before the present crisis, and as the crisis
has unfolded has gotten progressively easier. Today, America's real concern is inflation.
---
Mr. Gitlitz is chief economist at Trend Macrolytics, LLC.

--- Page 49 ---
Page 49 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
It's Time To Think Big On Tax Cuts
It's Time To Think Big On Tax Cuts
By Jack Kemp and Peter Ferrara
892 words
8 October 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A17
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
John McCain needs to show the nation that he has the economic recovery plan to restore long-term
economic growth. To do that, he needs to refocus his campaign with a new tax plan. Mr. McCain should
come out for an alternative, optional flatter tax system, which he has already supported.
Under this proposal, Americans could file their income taxes under the existing tax code or they could choose
instead to pay taxes under a simpler code with fewer deductions but lower tax rates. Building on work already
done by Steve Forbes and House Budget Committee member Paul Ryan, a Wisconsin Republican, Mr.
McCain could propose an optional tax system with just two rates, 10% and 25%, compared to the six rates of
the current code ranging from 10% to 35%.
What's more, such a proposal would include a cut in income taxes, and tax rates, for every American who
pays taxes. The alternative system would impose no income taxes on the poor and what is often called "the
working class" (the bottom 40% of income earners who don't pay federal income taxes now). This proposal
would also eliminate federal income taxes on the middle class, the middle 20% of income earners who pay
only 4.4% of all federal income taxes today.
The new tax system would allow most Americans to file their taxes on a single sheet of paper, saving them
the hundreds of dollars they spend today to have their taxes professionally prepared.
And such a tax reform would be an antidote to the class warfare, neo-collectivist tax policies of Barack
Obama. If implemented, it would also jump start the economy. Under this optional tax system, savings would
increase and investment would soar as capital around the world is drawn to a suddenly more confident U.S.
economy.
This new surge of capital would end the credit crunch, and allow old businesses to expand and new ones to
start. Wages would grow, along with the overall economy. And as the world invested in America, the dollar
would strengthen, as happened in response to the tax cuts that generated the 1980s Reagan boom. This
would ease inflationary fears and pressures on the Fed.
With a strong dollar, the Fed would be under less pressure to try to revive the economy through monetary
policy. That would give Mr. McCain the flexibility to push for a new "price rule," which would base monetary
policy on prices of a basket of commodities, including gold.
Mr. McCain has already proposed a freeze on nondefense, discretionary spending, and to limit overall federal
spending growth to 2.4%, about one-third the annual increases since 2000. Along with his trade policies, the
pro-growth tax rate cuts in this alternative tax system would give Mr. McCain all the major components --
lower taxes, freer trade, a strong dollar -- of Ronald Reagan's 1980 recovery plan.
Mr. McCain already has proposed several promising pro-growth tax reform plans that would lower taxes on
investment and the middle class. With American companies suffering the second-highest corporate tax rate in
the industrialized world, Mr. McCain would cut the federal corporate rate to 25% from 35%.
This would create jobs and increase wages, while boosting the dollar and raising revenue. A 2007 Treasury
Department study found that Ireland, with a 12.5% corporate rate, raises almost 50% more revenue as a
percent of GDP than the U.S. does with a 35% rate.
Mr. McCain is also already proposing to cut taxes on savings and investment through expensing for capital
equipment and technology investments -- allowing such expenses to be deducted in the year they are
incurred, like all other expenses, rather than over several years under arbitrary depreciation schedules as
today. This would do more for Detroit automakers than government loans.
He would prevent increases in individual income tax rates, capital gains tax rates, and dividend tax rates by
making the Bush rate cuts permanent, and cut the death tax to below pre-Bush levels.

--- Page 50 ---
Page 50 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. McCain wants to increase the dependent exemption to $7,000 from $3,500 per person today. This would
reduce taxes for middle-class families in the 25% income tax bracket by $875 per dependent. It would
probably eliminate most of the remaining federal income tax liability for the middle class by itself.
Mr. McCain also proposes to abolish the Alternative Minimum Tax (AMT), which is currently slated to grow to
impose a trillion dollar tax increase on the American people in just a few years, burdening 25 million
middle-class families. Abolishing the AMT would save middle-class families $2,700 on average per year, a
cut of $60 billion each year from current law.
Mr. Obama, by contrast, offers tax increases on savers, investors, small business, employers, and other job
creators, a trillion dollar plus spending increase, and new regulatory burdens. That has no prospect of
restoring economic growth. It will only do the opposite.
---
Mr. Kemp, chairman of Kemp Partners, is a nationally syndicated columnist. Mr. Ferrara is director of
entitlement and budget policy for the Institute for Policy Innovation and general counsel of the American Civil
Rights Union.

Dow Industrials Fall Below 10000 --- European Markets Also Swoon as Governments' Efforts Fail to
Allay Fears
By Peter A. McKay
580 words
7 October 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Even as stocks tumbled to multiyear lows, investors and traders were worried about more declines because
they saw few signs of capitulation -- or last-ditch selling that typically marks a market bottom.
Monday's 369.88-point, or 3.6%, drop in the Dow Jones Industrial Average, sending it below the 10000 mark
at 9955.50, didn't seem like a round of capitulation, said Doreen Mogavero, president and chief executive of
the New York floor brokerage Mogavero Lee &Co.
"Yes, it's a big move, but there hasn't been the sort of [trading] volume behind it that we'd like to see," in order
to confirm that there isn't another wave of sellers still waiting on the sidelines, Ms. Mogavero said.
The Dow closed below 10000 for the first time since Oct. 26, 2004. The Dow Jones Stoxx index of 600 big
European companies had its worst percentage drop in its 20-year history as emergency government
measures failed to stem concerns about the region's financial system and economy.
The U.S. government's $700 billion plan to bail out financial institutions didn't stop the Dow industrials from
falling about 800 points during the afternoon, to the lowest intraday level in bearly five years, although they
recovered some of those losses in the last hour of trading. All 30 Dow components declined, led by Bank of
America, which fell more than 6.5%.
The Dow has dropped 8.2% in the past four trading days, the largest four-day percentage decline in more
than six years. The blue-chip average is now 29.7% below its record hit last October.
The DJ Stoxx index finished down 7.6%, Germany's DAX was 7.1% lower, the U.K.'s FTSE-100 fell 7.9% and
the CAC-40 in Paris tumbled more than 9%.
The dollar rose 2.1% against the euro, which was at its lowest level since August 2007 in late New York
trading.
Oil futures tumbled 6.1% in New York, to $87.81 a barrel, the lowest end-of-day price since Feb. 6 and 40%
below its July 3 record as investors bet on a drop in demand for energy in a weak economy.
Trading in Russia was halted after the dollar-denominated RTS Index plunged 19.1%, its biggest-ever
one-day loss, and emerging markets elsewhere also tumbled. The Russian central bank was forced to use
$4 billion to $5 billion of its reserves to support the ruble amid the rout, traders said.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index slid 42.34 points, or 3.9%, to 1056.89, the lowest close in nearly five
years.The S&P is down 9.4% in the last four trading days, the largest four-day decline since July 2002.
With stocks falling, indicators of investor anxiety flashed higher. The Chicago Board Options Exchange's
Volatility Index, a key measure of investor fear, leapt 15.3% to 52.05.
Treasury prices rose as investors fled for safety. Gold futures jumped 4.1% to $862.70 per troy ounce.
"We're seeing pure fear right now," said Don Bright, of the Chicago proprietary firm Bright Trading. "My guys
who usually trade 5,000 shares at a time are now trading 1,000 or 2,000. They're a lot more skittish."

Market Slide Puts a Spotlight on Big Oil's Cash Hoard
Market Slide Puts a Spotlight on Big Oil's Cash Hoard
By Russell Gold
614 words
7 October 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Rising fears of a global economic downturn are sinking crude oil prices and driving down the share prices of
major oil companies despite the industry's record profits of the last two years.
Exxon Mobil Corp., the largest U.S. company and largest Western oil company by market capitalization, has
lost 17% of its share price since January, its worst showing since 1981. Its smaller peers are doing worse.
The stock prices of Chevron Corp., BP PLC, Royal Dutch Shell PLC, Total SA and ConocoPhillips, the largest
western oil companies, all hit new 52-week lows during the day on Monday.
The stock drops are driven by concerns that a world-wide recession will bring an end to the high oil prices
that have been the primary driver behind these companies' record earnings. The other main way Big Oil
boosts its profit -- oil and gas production -- "has not been growing," notes Credit Suisse analyst Mark
Flannery.
Historically, oil demand rises and falls with the economy. A global recession would slow or reverse demand
growth and deflate prices, pressuring oil companies to take one or more steps to boost their share prices.
Analysts say these include acquiring another company to boost growth, increasing share repurchases, or
offering a significant dividend increase.
Several of these companies, however, say they won't change course. A spokesman for Irving, Texas-based
Exxon says it is continuing on its long-term strategy of building value. "Shares do what the shares do," he
said. A spokesman for London-based BP said, "We don't manage the company day-to-day based on what's
happening to the share price." BP shares are down nearly 38% in the last 52 weeks.
The companies maintain brawny balance sheets, thanks to months of $100-plus oil prices, have ample cash
and are seen as good credit risks. Moreover, their investments have been made based on much lower oil
price assumptions. Unlike many smaller energy companies, they aren't compelled to shed assets or cut their
capital budgets to manage their cash. But sitting still isn't a permanent solution. Current, low interest rates
can mean poor returns on capital.
One large oil company that may need to change direction is ConocoPhillips. The Houston company needs to
deliver $5 billion this month to Australia's Origin Energy Ltd. under terms of a joint venture it entered into last
month to produce natural gas for export.
Mr. Flannery argues that Big Oil will need to put cash into acquisitions to restore the battered share prices. So
far this year, Exxon has lost $108 billion in market capitalization since peaking at $512.65 billion in January.
Others analysts contend that Exxon and its peers can wait for a fire sale by troubled companies.
Compared with many blue chips, Exxon, Chevron and other oil majors are cash-rich. Exxon has $39 billion in
cash and has been buying back shares at an $8 billion-a-quarter clip. The value of the stock it has
repurchased is about $218 billion, a shade less than the current value of General Electric Co.
One possibility mentioned by investors would be for Exxon and Chevron to increase their buybacks to
improve earnings per share. Energy analysts at Goldman Sachs and Merrill Lynch see longer-term oil market
prices as remaining strong, allowing oil companies to buy their own shares while those prices are low. But
this strategy is risky as oil companies have become political targets in the presidential campaign for not doing
enough to boost supplies of oil and gas.

Ben Casselman contributed to this article.

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
By Jeff D. Opdyke
548 words
6 October 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Prices Drop,
Taking Spirits
With Them
From oil and gold to stocks and houses, prices these days are heading in one direction. To a certain extent,
that's OK; prices moderate after bubbles burst. And falling prices do have some positive effects on the
economy.
But in the context of the increasingly tight credit crunch, declining prices can race toward dreaded deflation.
At issue is a "systemic deleveraging," or the paying down of massive debts this country has accumulated in
recent decades. That's a naturally deflationary force, and one set to "go on for years inside the U.S." and
Europe, says Christian Blaabjerg, market strategist at Saxo Bank, a European investment bank. Households,
corporations and investors are selling off assets to reduce debt. Banks aren't eager to repeat lending
mistakes of the past. Reduced consumer credit is likely. That will shrink spending.
Some businesses will cut prices to attract buyers, who, seeing prices fall, will reflexively await further
declines.
"During the last 15 years, people thought we had no inflation, but we did," says Doug Noland, market
strategist at David W. Tice &Assoc., a Dallas investment firm long predicting today's woes and known for
betting against the market. Wall Street's finance bubble funded asset-price inflation and spending on luxury
goods, Mr. Noland says, and "all those prices are now deflating." Deflation can lead to a vicious spiral of
business closures, layoffs and more of the same.
Not everyone agrees that deflation is imminent. Neal Soss, Credit Suisse's chief economist, says "rising asset
prices didn't force inflation up, so why should deleverarging necessarily force [consumer prices] down? I see
a very low rate of inflation, but I don't see deflation."
Actions by the Federal Reserve to revive the economy also are more likely to prove inflationary. The bailout
Congress approved means the Fed will likely print dollars, and it has already pumped in hundreds of billions
to lubricate the financial system. Both moves are inflationary. If global investors eventually demand higher
rates to accept risk, inflation heats up. The dollar, now strong, looks to resume its bearish bent, jacking up
prices on imports, another inflationary push.
The overall result is likely to be a temporary inflationary spike. But other data point to further declines in
real-estate prices, restrictive credit and slowing consumption, which is ultimately deflationary.
Asset-price deflation is already clear. The housing market is in freefall, and Case-Shiller's 20 City Composite
Home Price Index recently posted a record decline. Prices for non-Treasury bonds, including municipals, are
falling. Oil is down, but that could moderate since China and India will continue to grow, though at a slower
pace.
Ultimately, this economic patch will pass -- but like a kidney stone.
With corporate-earnings growth expected to shrink, the stock market has further to fall. In a deflationary
world, cash is king. Gold, too, should eventually fare well since it's not a fiat currency. Otherwise, keep your
head down awhile.

--- Page 55 ---
Page 55 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"We're in a national crisis," says Harley Lance Kaplan, a financial adviser in Sherborn, Mass. Still, he adds,
"this deleveraging is absolutely essential. We're clearly overleveraged as a society."

The Weekend Interview with Asif Ali Zardari: The Most Difficult Job in the World
The Weekend Interview with Asif Ali Zardari: The Most Difficult Job in the World
By Bret Stephens
1,863 words
4 October 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Asif Ali Zardari used to sport a full moustache, jet black and rakish in the style of the avid polo player he once
was. But sometime in the past year he trimmed it short and let its salt-and-pepper colors show. It befits the
sober role he has now assumed, at 53, as the president of Pakistan, probably the world's most difficult -- and
dangerous -- political job.
Mr. Zardari shows no signs that he is stepping into that role diffidently. In an interview last Saturday with The
Wall Street Journal, held under tight security at a midtown Manhattan hotel, he crafted his phrases in a tone
of command. Pakistan's war, he says, is "my war," its fighter jets "my F-16s," its Intelligence Bureau "my IB."
When he discusses Pakistan's economic crisis -- the central bank has about two months' worth of foreign
currency reserves left to pay for the country's imports of oil and food -- he says he looks to the world to "give
me $100 billion."
For a man who has been president for less than a month, that's an ambitious request -- all the more given his
checkered past. Mr. Zardari is, of course, the widower of Benazir Bhutto, the former two-time Pakistani prime
minister assassinated last December shortly after her tumultuous return from an eight-year exile. He invokes
her name repeatedly throughout our interview, at times to stress the importance he attaches to women's
rights and empowerment, at other times to underline how personally he takes the threat of Islamic radicalism.
But Mr. Zardari is also known as "Mr. Ten Percent," a moniker he acquired thanks to his legendary reputation
for graft. At one time or another, he and his late wife were suspected of profiting (or seeking to profit) from
corrupt schemes involving everything from the purchase of Polish tractors and French jets to the import of
gold bullion. In 2006, he even produced a diagnosis of dementia from two New York psychiatrists as part of
an effort to defend himself in a corruption case in Britain.
These days, Mr. Zardari seems to be in excellent mental health -- if indeed he was ever unwell. Nor does he
seem particularly vexed by his own past notoriety: All charges against him were eventually dropped in a
political deal the previous government of President Pervez Musharraf struck with Bhutto, and as president he
enjoys legal immunity. As for the broader corruption concerns, he all but waves them away as irrelevant. The
corruption issue, he says, "has been used for a long time as a political tool," particularly by "radicals" trying to
sully democracy's good name. Foreign investors, he adds, have been coming to Pakistan for decades, and
"none of them have complained about corruption."
That last observation may come as news to at least a few investors -- Pakistan ranked near the bottom of
Transparency International's corruption perception index in 1995, the last full year during which Bhutto was in
power. Investors might also have memories of the circumstances in which Bhutto's second government
collapsed in 1996.
"Since her re-election to office in 1993, [Ms. Bhutto] has run roughshod over strict fiscal and economic targets
laid out by the International Monetary Fund for Pakistan's anemic economy," wrote Journal reporter Peter
Waldman in November 1996. "In one of her more perplexing moves, she kept the Finance Ministry portfolio
for herself, making it virtually impossible for her coalition government to muster the political will to curb
Pakistan's gaping budget deficit. The lack of confidence in her government recently reached crisis
proportions, with Pakistan's foreign-currency reserves plummeting below $700 million, or less than a single
month's imports." At the time, Mr. Zardari occupied the post of Pakistan's minister of investments, reporting to
his wife.
Put simply, the economic crisis Mr. Zardari faces today is, at least in part, a crisis of confidence in him. He
alludes to this problem only once in the interview, noting that before he can hope to get foreign help he will
"have to make my credibility, my case." Still, he has a simple and powerful argument to make that the world
cannot allow his government to fail -- not when it's becoming increasingly plausible that Pakistan itself, with its
stockpile of as many as 200 nuclear warheads, could be toppled by al Qaeda and its allies.

--- Page 57 ---
Page 57 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"I need your help," he says more than once. "If we fall, if we can't do it, you can't do it."
In asking for the help -- and $100 billion is no small request, even (or particularly) in the age of AIG -- Mr.
Zardari is keen to insist that it not be described as aid. "Aid is proven through the researches of the World
Bank . . . [to be] bad for a country," he says. "I'm looking for temporary relief for my budgetary support and
cash for my treasury which does not need to be spent by me. It is not something I want to spend. But [it] will
stop the [outflow] of my capital every time there is a bomb. . . . In this situation, how do I create capital
confidence, how do I create businessmen's confidence?"
To his credit, Mr. Zardari's answer involves more than simply passing around the collection plate. When I ask
whether he would consider a free-trade agreement with traditional archenemy India, Mr. Zardari responds
with a string of welcome, perhaps even historic, surprises. "India has never been a threat to Pakistan," he
says, adding that "I, for one, and our democratic government is not scared of Indian influence abroad." He
speaks of the militant Islamic groups operating in Kashmir as "terrorists" -- former President Musharraf would
more likely have called them "freedom fighters" -- and allows that he has no objection to the India-U.S.
nuclear cooperation pact, so long as Pakistan is treated "at par." "Why would we begrudge the largest
democracy in the world getting friendly with one of the oldest democracies in the world?"
Not only does Mr. Zardari want better ties with Delhi, he notes that "there is no other economic survival for
nations like us. We have to trade with our neighbors first." He imagines Pakistani cement factories being
constructed to provide for India's huge infrastructure needs, Pakistani textile mills meeting Indian demand for
blue jeans, Pakistani ports being used to relieve the congestion at Indian ones. For a country that spent most
of its existence trying to show that it's the military equal of its neighbor, the agenda amounts to a remarkable
recognition of the strides India has made in becoming a true world power.
But before Pakistan can hope to save itself by completely reshaping the situation on its eastern frontier, it has
the more pressing problem of resolving the crisis to its west, in its tribal areas along the border with
Afghanistan. On the day of our meeting, there had been reports that Pakistani army forces had fired on U.S.
aircraft operating along the border with Afghanistan, while Pakistani officials were taking an ever-tougher line
against NATO commando raids against the Taliban on Pakistani soil.
Mr. Zardari seems anxious to downplay any differences with the U.S. "I am not going to fall for this position
that it's an unpopular thing to be an American friend. I am an American friend." The firing on the U.S. aircraft
was, he says, merely an incident, "and while incidents do happen, they are not important." He goes off the
record to describe sensitive military subjects, but acknowledges that the U.S. is carrying out Predator missile
strikes on Pakistani soil with his government's consent. "We have an understanding, in the sense that we're
going after an enemy together."
He also acknowledges the problem that had bedeviled past efforts at U.S.-Pakistani cooperation, particularly
in intelligence sharing: the widely held suspicion that Pakistani intelligence services continue to cooperate
with, and even arm, the Taliban. "You know, you keep an uglier alternative around so that you may not be
asked to leave," he says, in reference to Mr. Musharraf's habit of fighting Islamic radicals with one hand while
protecting them with the other. Mr. Zardari refuses to go into further detail other than to say he "solved the
problem"; the head of Pakistani intelligence was fired earlier this week.
Mr. Zardari seems to hope that, with the intelligence problem out of the way, a new era of cooperation can
open up with the U.S. "We want to be able to share [U.S.] intelligence," he says. "We need helicopters, we
need night goggles, we need equipment of that sort." He stresses the need for precision and finesse in
fighting Islamic militants, rather than large-scale military force. "My eventual concept is that we should be
taking them on as they are, as criminals." Of Osama bin Laden he says, "the minute I make anybody my
enemy, he becomes as big as I am."
In recent weeks there have been reports that Pakistan has deployed F-16s against tribal insurgents, in part
because the army's own frontier troops have been routinely routed in ground fighting. Their problems aren't
simply tactical. "What kind of a joke is this that I cannot pay my security personnel more than the Talibs are
paying?" he asks. "Those terrorists are paying their soldiers 10,000 rupees; I'm paying seven or six thousand
rupees."
The effects of such a disparity are increasingly in evidence. The recent bombing of Islamabad's Marriott hotel,
in an area that is under particularly tight security controls, is a fresh reminder that Pakistan's terrorist problem
extends well beyond the tribal hinterlands.
Speaking of the attack, Mr. Zardari again brings the subject around to his economic problem. "If I can't pay
my own oil bill, how am I going to increase my police?" he asks. "The oil companies are asking me to pay
$135 [per barrel] of oil and at the same time they want me to keep the world peaceful and Pakistan peaceful."

--- Page 58 ---
Page 58 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
It's a fair point. And it leads Mr. Zardari to a kind of peroration, the case he has to make that he is, after all,
the right man for Pakistan in its hour of peril -- however improbable that may seem given everything that is
known or suspected about his past.
"You know, every life has its end," he says. "So, before mine ends, I want to finish this job and I want them to
remember that they did get my wife and I won't let them get away with it. I do not necessarily feel that death is
a reality. I do not deny death. But the way they did it, they killed the mother of my children so it's very
personal for me. And before I finish, when my life ends, I need this job done. The sooner the better."
---
Mr. Stephens writes "Global View," the Journal's foreign-affairs column.

Led by Gold, Commodities Fall Sharply --- Stronger Dollar, Economic Woes Hit Oil, Soybeans
Commodities Report
Led by Gold, Commodities Fall Sharply --- Stronger Dollar, Economic Woes Hit Oil, Soybeans
By Allen Sykora
387 words
3 October 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A need to raise cash and sharp gains by the dollar triggered massive selling in gold futures, sending prices
skidding nearly 5%.
Investors remain anxious over the health of the U.S. financial system and continued to withdraw funds from
commodities in general to free-up cash. Fears about long-term global economic weakness crimping
raw-material demand also weakened prices across the entire commodity spectrum.
The gold market was particularly illustrative of investor concerns. Nearby October gold lost $41.70, or 4.7%,
to $839 a troy ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. Most-active December
lost $43, or 4.8%, to $844.30 an ounce.
George Gero, vice president with RBC Capital Markets Global Futures said, "What you have is the need for
cash from the prime brokers and the hedge funds.
"They need the cash for other markets where they are getting margin calls," he said.
The other driving force behind the commodity slump was the U.S. currency. The euro fell to $1.3748, its
weakest level in a year. The move occurred after European Central Bank President Jean-Claude Trichet
said inflation risks have diminished, increasing expectations for a rate cut.
A strong dollar is often bearish for commodities because it makes them more expensive to foreign buyers.
The economic situation weighed heavily on crude-oil futures, which slid 4.6% to nearly an eight-month low.
Nymex November crude settled at $93.97 a barrel, down $4.56.
The potential rate cut comes amid the threat of a recession in the euro zone, which would result in weaker oil
demand.
The weakness in crude oil contributed to losses in other commodities, such as grains, overpowering
individual market fundamentals.
At the Chicago Board of Trade, nearby December corn fell 30 cents -- the maximum move allowed by the
exchange -- to settle at $4.54 a bushel. That represents a 6% drop.
CBOT wheat and soybeans also fell because of the influence of crude oil and the world's economic ills. CBOT
December wheat fell 33.75 cents to $6.36 a bushel, and November soybeans dropped 49 cents to $10.04 a
bushel.

Dow Falls 348.22, as Pressure Dogs Insurers --- Recession Worries Trump Bailout Hope; 'A Vortex of Selling'
Thursday's Markets
Dow Falls 348.22, as Pressure Dogs Insurers --- Recession Worries Trump Bailout Hope; 'A Vortex of
Selling'
By Peter A. McKay
590 words
3 October 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Markets tumbled again as increasing evidence of recession overshadowed hopes that a government plan to
shore up the financial system would be approved by Congress.
Stocks and commodities slid, prices for Treasury securities rose sharply, banks remained wary of lending and
European currencies slumped amid worries that economic weakness there will deepen.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average plummeted 348.22 points, or 3.2%, to end at 10482.85, just 117 points
above its Monday close, which followed the biggest one-day point decline in its history. The Dow is down
21% in 2008, with less than three months to go in the year.
The blue-chip measure was hurt Thursday by a 9.6% slide in its component General Electric ahead of pricing
of the company's $12 billion public offering. All but four of the 30 Dow stocks posted declines.
"What we have now is a vortex of selling that just feeds on itself. Hedge funds are tripping over other hedge
funds to get out," said Doug Kass, president of hedge fund Seabreeze Partners Management. "The economy
appears to have fallen off a cliff in September."
New data showed a seven-year high in jobless claims and a plunge in factory orders, underscoring woes in
nonfinancial sectors of the economy. Friday's report from the Labor Department is expected to report a big
decline in nonfarm payrolls in September.
The Dow Jones Transportation Average fell 8.7%, its biggest decline since the day the stock market
reopened after the terrorist attacks of 2001, on concerns that there won't be much need to move goods
around as the U.S. economy weakens.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 46.78 points, or 4%, to 1114.28. All its sectors ended lower, led by
the most economically sensitive categories. Basic materials slid 8.4%, industrials were off 7.2%, and energy
was down 6.2%.
Stocks that declined outpaced those that rose by a ratio of more than five to one. Prices of raw materials
were hit hard amid fears of sagging demand because of the global economy's weakness. Crude-oil futures
fell 4.6% to $93.97 a barrel in New York. Gold contracts suffered their worst drop since March, falling 4.7% to
$839 per troy ounce in New York. The broad Dow Jones-AIG Commodity Index shed almost 4.5%.
Investors fled to Treasurys as a safe haven. The yield on three-month bills fell to around 0.6%, down from
about 0.8% late Wednesday.
The U.S. commercial-paper market, which banks and other companies use to fund short-term obligations
such as payrolls and inventories, remained under pressure. The decline in the amount of commercial paper
outstanding in September was the largest drop since the Federal Reserve began tracking such data in 1991.
Although investors were hopeful that a financial-rescue plan would be approved by the House after passage
Wednesday night by the Senate, they are increasingly coming to the bleak realization that at best, it would
only minimize, not reverse, the economic pain that lies ahead.
"We've been focusing on the bailout so much in the last two weeks, it's almost like we haven't been paying
attention to the economic signs that would usually be a big deal for us," said Art Hogan, chief market
strategist at Jefferies &Co. "The economic data look atrocious."

When Stocks Tank, Some Investors Stampede to Alpacas and Turn to Drink --- Parking Space Is Real-Estate Investment; Can Comic-Book Index...
When Stocks Tank, Some Investors Stampede to Alpacas and Turn to Drink --- Parking Space Is
Real-Estate Investment; Can Comic-Book Index Outpace the Market?
By Jennifer Levitz
1,060 words
3 October 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Who can blame an investor for taking to the bottle?
Andy Pick, a 49-year-old stay-at-home father in Atlanta, recently bypassed the stock market for liquid assets
-- $120,000 in champagnes. He bought 400 bottles, mostly 1996 vintage, that he says he plans to "sit on" for
10 or 15 years and then sell at a profit.
"It sure beats looking at a Merrill Lynch monthly statement," he says, adding, "The worst thing that could
happen is that I drink all of it."
Given the gyrations in the financial markets, some investors are abandoning stocks and bonds and seeking
refuge in unusual alternatives -- parking spaces, for instance, and condos in Peru. Sales of exotic livestock
are up. The U.S. Mint has seen a gold-coin rush.
Investors have long turned to hard assets in market downturns, the idea being that if you invest in something
real, it won't disappear, even if its value declines. But analysts say this downturn is different in that real
estate, the most traditional safe haven, is also sinking. Between July 2006 and July this year, home prices
dropped 19.5%, according to the S&P/Case-Shiller 20-city composite home price index.
After the market dropped in January, Steve Borter, the 56-year-old president of a heating-and-air-conditioning
company, did invest in real estate, but not the usual sort. He became landlord of a single parking space in
Chicago. He bought a 12-by-20-foot spot in the Field Harbor Parking Garage for $29,000 and rents it out.
"The stock market is indicative of a lot of uncertainty. With a parking space, at least you end up with
something," he says.
Peggy Parks, a 49-year-old auditor in Johnstown, Pa., turned to an unusual farm animal. "I've lost a fortune in
stocks, and my 401(k) is falling through the floor. I feel comfortable in alpacas," she says. She invested
$56,000 in a small herd that she believes has a better outlook than most mutual funds because of the
animals' breeding potential.
The national Alpaca Registry Inc., in Lincoln, Neb., says registrations are on pace to rise 7% this year and
currently stand at 140,297. Ms. Parks says a female of "medium quality" can fetch $10,000 and that prices
have been rising, supporting her hopes that she'll see a profit on her alpaca portfolio in five years.
Financial firms are reporting that a growing number of retirees are rolling their money out of ordinary
individual retirement accounts -- commonly stocks, bonds and mutual funds -- and into self-directed IRAs,
where almost anything goes. "We've had people invest in a cypress farm in Costa Rica, and a condo in
Croatia," says Tom Anderson, president of Pensco Inc., a San Francisco firm that has $3.3 billion in
self-directed IRAs under custody. He says 20% more assets flowed in over the past three months than in the
same period a year ago.
In Centennial, Colo., Tim Boykin, 56, a retired engineer, says he pulled his entire nest egg of nearly $1 million
out of stock and bond funds in August and put it into a self-directed IRA. He invested some of the money in
his niece's company -- which is building condos in Lima, Peru. While analysts warn that real-estate
investments in emergng markets are risky, Mr. Boykin says he has done his research and remains confident:
"I can see pictures of the land. I can see steel. I can see people working. When I put my money in a fund, I
see a big list of things that don't sound good."
Not everyone thinks alternative investments are a great idea. The Alabama Securities Commission over the
weekend issued an "investor alert" urging caution. People are "panicking," says securities director Joseph
Borg. He worries that investors who yank their money out of the stock market are prey for con artists hawking
things like phantom oil wells.

--- Page 63 ---
Page 63 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Borg, past president of the North American Securities Administrators Association, adds that in past
market downturns he saw people turn to chinchillas, worm farms and super-breeds of rabbits. Emus, too,
were big. "Eventually, people got tired of them and just let them go," he says. "To this day, you'll be in West
Texas and a big emu running wild will just come up next to your car."
Hard-asset gurus like Howard Ruff, a best-selling author who rose to fame in the inflationary 1970s, are
convinced their moment has come again. "This is a big, big time, a very big time -- and this is just the
beginning," says Mr. Ruff. He has been advising people to buy bags of pre-1965 U.S dimes and quarters,
which are 90% silver and in limited supply.
Gold coins also are in great demand. Last week, the mint suspended sales of American Buffalo 24-karat
gold coins because it can't keep up with soaring sales. Last month, a record 14,000 bidders -- 17% more
than the previous high -- turned out for a coin-and-currency auction in Long Beach, Calif., that generated $35
million in sales.
Bob Sale, a Blue Bunny brand ice-cream distributor in Colorado Springs, Colo., says he purchased American
Eagle gold coins last week after his 401(k) retirement account tanked. "Holding them in your hand is like no
other feeling," he says.
Mark Craddock, manager of Comic Book World, in Florence, Ky., says stock-market investors also are
turning to superheroes. "There's kind of a buying frenzy" in vintage comic books, he says.
The "Silver Age Comic Book Pricing Index" of 32 frequently traded '60s comics, was up 14.2% in the 18
months ending in July, while the Standard &Poor's 500 stock index was down 11% in the same period. Mark
Haspel, president of Certified Guaranty Co. in Sarasota, Fla., which grades comic books, often for investors,
says it's on track to handle 200,000 books this year, up from 150,000 in 2007.
"Spiderman is going to be here in 20 years -- he's not going away," Mr. Haspel says.
