Even with few stocks left to short, traders still found a way to sell small caps Monday.
In an overarching move late last week, the Securities and Exchange Commission passed a ruling to stem
short-selling of nearly 800 financial firms. That helped to propel a broad late-week rally for small caps, which
for the purposes of this column are those with a market cap of $2 billion or less.
And though that rule was extended to even more companies on Monday, much of Friday's gains evaporated
as the Bush administration's $700 billion plan to save financial markets was panned in some parts of
Monday's trade. (A short sale is the sale of borrowed securities by an investor who hopes to make a profit by
buying an equal number of shares later at a lower price to replace the borrowed ones.)
"There is an obvious inflationary element to all of this: Look at gold and oil [on Monday]. People are reading
that we're going to shovel a lot of money into the system and it's going to create inflation," said Jon
Merriman, chief executive of boutique investment bank and brokerage firm Merriman Curhan Ford.
Gold closed the session up 5%, while oil prices tacked on 16%, marking the biggest one-day gain ever. And
with a return to surging commodities prices, consumer stocks sold off aggressively, including a tumble of $2,
or 29%, to $4.85, for home builder Standard Pacific on the New York Stock Exchange.
The Russell 2000 index of small-capitalization stocks declined 33.30 points, or 4.42%, to 720.44, marking its
second-biggest drop of the year.
The Standard &Poor's SmallCap 600 dropped 16.88 points, or 4.25%, to 380.46.
Those declines wiped away all of Friday's 4.15% gain for the Russell and 3.39% for the S&P 600.
While the inflationary aspect of the government's moves helped to drive up commodities prices and weigh on
consumer stocks, the bailout was also met with a concern among investors in financial stocks. Though the
moves are seen as helping to stabilize the increasingly volatile space, there remains skepticism over how
banks will operate in a more regulated environment.
David Klaskin, chief investment officer for Oak Ridge Investments in Chicago, said the rules have created a
new unlevered business environment that will most likely mean that, for many years, banks won't see
double-digit percentage growth in earnings.
"There are some financial institutions that will benefit from having things stabilize a little bit, but that big move
up on Thursday that continued on Friday was unrealistic," he said.
Among the financials, commercial banks were the most hard-hit on Monday, including Sterling Bancshares,
off 3.02, or 22%, at 10.98, and East West Bancorp, down 3.72, or 21%, to 13.93.
With the gain for oil, airlines and other transport companies finished well into the red Monday. Leading the
decliners, Chicago-based United Airlines parent UAL lost 1.44, or 11%, to 11.80.
Armonk, N.Y.-based MBIA extended Friday's declines that were caused by Moody's Investors Service saying
it may again downgrade the bond insurer's units. MBIA reiterated on Monday it has enough cash to fund
potential termination payments in the event of a downgrade. For the session, MBIA lost 73 cents, or 5.7%, to
12.15.
Fellow bond insurer Ambac Financial sagged 36 cents, or 9.3%, to 3.51, after Moody's said it could
downgrade the New York firm.

Crude Oil Records Biggest One-Day Gain, While Dollar Slides --- Hurricane Fallout, Mistimed Bears Lift Expiring Pact 16%
Commodities Report
Crude Oil Records Biggest One-Day Gain, While Dollar Slides --- Hurricane Fallout, Mistimed Bears
Lift Expiring Pact 16%
By Ann Davis and Neil King Jr.
895 words
23 September 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Reaction to the Wall Street bailout and frenzied last-minute trading in the oil market sent crude prices soaring
by more than $16 a barrel, the biggest one-day jump ever.
The late-day spike, which shoved oil up 16% to $120.92 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange,
offered an illustration of Wall Street's hard-to-predict moves amid broad market turmoil. The nation's
futures-market regulator announced it would probe the move for evidence of illegal manipulation. The jump
confounded experts and bore the markings of a run for the exits by a major player.
Traders blamed the spike on one or more players who had been bearish on oil getting caught in what is
known as a "short squeeze." In such a situation, a trader who has previously agreed to sell oil at a much
lower price than where the market has risen faces a choice: Either post significant collateral to stay in the
game, or enter into offsetting contracts to buy oil at much higher prices to cancel out the bet. But Monday, the
contract for oil for October delivery was expiring, and so the player may have had only one choice if he didn't
want to make delivery of the oil: Unwind the bad bet.
"This was just a short squeeze of absolute epic proportions. There are absolutely no fundamentals to justify
where we were today," said Stephen Schork, author of a daily energy-market report and a former Nymex floor
trader.
The move showed how precarious sentiment is in the oil markets, as they are buffeted by shifts in other
financial markets as the government response to the credit crisis takes multiple twists. On Sept. 16, oil for
October delivery traded below $92, closing at $91.15. It traded as much as 43% higher than that Monday,
hitting $130 a barrel seven minutes before the market's close at 2:30 p.m. EDT. Nymex set the final price at
$120.92 a barrel, up $16.37 on the day.
Because of the late-day spike, traders expect oil prices to fall Tuesday morning, when all attention turns to
the November contract. The October contract closed $11.55 higher than the contract for delivery in
November, another indication the rally in the near-term October contract represented a squeeze.
In any case, the unsettled stock market and a suddenly weakened dollar are giving fresh buoyancy to the oil
market and other raw materials. Energy, metals and agriculture futures moved broadly higher Monday, an
indication that investors worried about the U.S. bailout and currency again sought commodities as a store of
value.
While many analysts thought a week ago that oil could flatten out at about $90 a barrel or lower, many now
say an uptick in demand in the U.S. and concerns about inflation could keep oil above $110 for much of the
fourth quarter. They also note signs within the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries that suppliers
such as Saudi Arabia are beginning to trim output amid nervousness within the cartel that prices could get
soft.
While it was a bullish day for energy markets, the contracts for gasoline, heating oil and natural gas rallied far
less. Gasoline futures closed at $2.7038 a gallon, up 10.41 cents, or 4%, heating oil closed at $3.043 a
gallon, up 14.52 cents, or 5%. Natural gas closed at $7.658 per million British thermal units, up 12.7 cents, or
1.7%.
Analysts said Monday's spike hinted at a large player such as an energy company that had physical oil
inventories that it may have hedged by selling oil at a much lower price, although it also could be a hedge
fund or other financial institution in distress. Oil trading is highly leveraged; oil contracts are typically entered
into by depositing 5% to 10% of the total notional value with a clearing firm that guarantees the trades on the

--- Page 115 ---
Page 115 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
exchange. But if the market goes in the opposite direction of the bet, multiples of that amount may be due as
margin to keep the clearing firm from closing out the trade.
Because the October contract expired Monday, a trade-clearing firm that had a client with a wrong-way bet
may have forced the client to liquidate the contract. Another theory: Since the spike was in the 30-minute
window in which oil closing prices are calculated, a trader might have chosen that period to unwind the trade
in order to more directly influence the final closing price, a potentially improper move. Nymex wouldn't
comment on whether it suspects any infractions or errors in Monday's trading.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Investors fretting about the financial-sector bailout flocked to gold again, sending it up $43.30 to
$903.90 an ounce on the Comex division of Nymex.
SOYBEANS: Prices on the Chicago Board of Trade soared on inflationary-based commodity buying.
November soybeans added 61.5 cents to $12.05 a bushel.

Central Banks Consider Gold
Commodities Report
Central Banks Consider Gold
By Andrea Hotter and Matt Whittaker
530 words
22 September 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Central banks may be starting to turn to one of the few assets in which they can invest: gold.
Turbulence in the financial markets and recent U.S. dollar weakness are helping the precious metal claw
back its reputation as the central monetary anchor within the international monetary framework, industry
participants say.
This is a marked change from a decade ago, when 14 European central banks decided to reduce their gold
holdings in an orderly fashion. They signed a pact to sell no more than an agreed sum between them each
year. In addition, the U.S., the Bank for International Settlements and the International Monetary Fund adhere
to the pact informally.
But the amount sold has been dwindling of late, and banks are now far more likely to be holders of gold,
analysts note. Nonsignatories -- and especially Asian banks -- are seen as keen buyers.
"I'd be surprised if the Chinese hadn't been nibbling at the gold market when prices were lower," said Mark
O'Byrne, Dublin-based director of Gold &Silver Investments Ltd.
Chinese officials have already said they view gold as a strategic asset and would like to diversify their
foreign-exchange reserves away from the dollar. China holds just 1% of its reserves in gold, World Gold
Council data show, equivalent to roughly 600 metric tons. Even so, it is the ninth-largest official holder of gold.
The U.S. is at the top of the list of official holders. It holds 78.2% of its reserves in gold, which is about
8,133.5 tons of gold.
There is debate on how much, if any, central-bank buying was done last week, when gold saw a record
single-day gain Wednesday and posted another outsized rise Thursday before pulling back Friday. For the
week, thinly traded nearby September gold on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange
jumped $100.30, or 13%, to settle at $860.60 an ounce. Most-active December also gained 13% on the
week, to $864.70.
"We could not see a footprint from a central bank," said Jon Nadler, an analyst at Kitco Bullion Dealers in
Montreal. The Swiss central bank doesn't appear to have been buying back what it recently sold, he said.
But investors bought for much of the week as they searched for a refuge from financial-market turmoil.
Friday's decline came as the U.S. government worked on a plan to take over troubled financial assets.
But even central banks that signed on to the sales program are starting to publicly state the value of gold in
their portfolios.
The Austrian central bank said in a recent report that the surge in gold prices and "the concomitant
depreciation of the U.S. dollar over the past few years have shown clearly how important gold is as an
instrument for portfolio diversification for a central bank."
Germany's Bundesbank, the world's second-largest official holder of gold with 3,417.4 tons, or 66.3% of its
reserves, has indicated it is more willing to hold or buy gold than before.

Oil Forecasters See Higher Prices in 2009
Commodities Report
Oil Forecasters See Higher Prices in 2009
By Gregory Meyer
853 words
20 September 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Oil's recent tumble has forced analysts to rip up price forecasts, but they aren't backing off
arguments that higher prices are ahead.
The caveats are plentiful: Some say oil could fall as low as $75 a barrel if the global economy crashes,
another warns of $90 oil should the dollar continue to strengthen. But overall, the forecasts point to a barrel of
crude commanding a bigger sum next year than now, with analysts largely citing recovering demand and
supply shortfalls to support their view.
After stumbling as low as $90.51 a barrel Tuesday, a seven-month low, the oil-futures market has offered
some vindication to the bulls, bouncing above $105 a barrel Friday. Front-month light, sweet crude for
October delivery on the New York Mercantile Exchange settled at $104.55 a barrel, up $6.67, or 6.8%.
The latest -- and most prominent -- analyst to chop his oil forecast was Arjun Murti, who covers oil companies
as an equity analyst at Goldman Sachs. Mr. Murti three years ago predicted oil would reach $105 a barrel, a
call that seemed outrageous at the time but proved to be correct and gained him a modicum of fame in the
process. This past week, Mr. Murti said oil is likely to average $110 a barrel next year, trimming $30 from his
prior forecast.
His downgrade and others' follow a fall from $147.27 a barrel in mid-July prompted by softening demand in
wealthier countries, new supplies from the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries and a stronger
dollar. Mr. Murti's colleagues on the Goldman Sachs commodities-research desk also lowered their forecasts
and now see crude at $123 a barrel next year, from $148 previously.
Barclays Capital this month pulled down its 2009 price forecast to $115.50 a barrel from $123.20, or 6.3%, as
demand sags and the market's dour sentiment proves hard to stop, at least in the short term.
Others have stood by their forecasts. At Merrill Lynch, analyst Francisco Blanch sees crude averaging $107 a
barrel next year. Deutsche Bank, sees crude at $120 a barrel next year.
Mr. Murti expects three factors to restore vigor in the oil market during the fourth quarter: a post-Olympics
demand rebound in China; stronger U.S. demand owing to cheaper prices; and the need to replenish oil
inventories, which he says were pressured by Hurricanes Gustav and Ike.
"Our bullish oil-macro framework has been based on the fairly straightforward logic that in an environment of
limited spare capacity and lackluster supply growth, prices would need to rise to ration demand down to
levels of available supply," Mr. Murti said in a note this past week. "We believe the essence of this call
remains intact."
The most bullish of the pack share a fundamental belief that demand will put supplies to the test. But they
quibble on the details. Mr. Murti is the chief exponent of what he calls a "super-spike" in oil prices caused by
lackluster and disruption-prone supply growth. In May, he wrote there was increasing likelihood crude could
reach $150 to $200 a barrel in the next six to 24 months.
The Barclays team, led by Paul Horsnell, scoff at the super-spike hypothesis even if they agree with most of
the conclusions. As they amended their price outlook, they cheered that the "correction down should mark a
temporary end to the interminable interviews with the false prophets of the so-called supercycle."
Not everyone sees triple-digit oil prices lasting. Lehman Brothers analysts, headed by Chief Energy
Economist Edward Morse, forecast prices at $93 a barrel for 2009 amid a demand dropoff in advanced
industrial economies, a slowing Chinese economy, a surge of supply from OPEC, oil sands and offshore
projects.

--- Page 119 ---
Page 119 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Lehman's parent filed for bankruptcy-court protection Monday and its commodities business is now set to
become part of Barclays Capital. It is unclear whether Lehman's oil team will be integrated into Barclays's,
and if so, how they will reconcile their dueling views.
One of the boldest forecasts comes from an entity that isn't a Wall Street bank at all: the U.S. government.
The Energy Information Administration, the independent statistics and analysis unit of the Department of
Energy, earlier this month raised its outlook in spite of oil's recent slide. The EIA now sees crude at $126.50 a
barrel next year because of "projected stronger growth in world petroleum demand."

COPPER: Prices rose amid strong gains in U.S. stocks and a rally in crude-oil futures prices. Comex
September copper gained 8.95 cents a pound, or 2.9%, to $3.1850.

Mounting Fears Shake World Markets As Banking Giants Rush to Raise Capital
Mounting Fears Shake World Markets As Banking Giants Rush to Raise Capital
By Tom Lauricella, Liz Rappaport and Annelena Lobb
1,323 words
18 September 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Fear coursed through the U.S. financial system on Wednesday, as hope for a resolution to the year-old credit
crisis faded.
Stocks tumbled, concern grew about which financial firm would fall next, and investors rushed toward the
safe haven of government bonds in the wake of the collapse of Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc. and the crisis
at insurer American International Group.
The market turmoil is doing more than inflicting losses on investors. Borrowing costs for U.S. companies have
skyrocketed, and the debt markets have become nearly inaccessible to all but the most creditworthy
borrowers.
The desperation was especially striking in the market for U.S. government debt, long considered the safest of
investments. At one point during the day, investors were willing to pay more for one-month Treasurys than
they could expect to get back when the bonds matured. Some investors, in essence, had decided that a small
but known loss was better than the uncertainty connected to any other type of investment.
That's never happened before. In a special government auction on Wednesday, demand ran so high that the
Treasury Department sold $40 billion in bills, far beyond what it needed to cover the government's
obligations.
"We've seen crisis. We've seen recession. But we've not seen the core of the financial system shaken like
this," says Joseph Balestrino, a portfolio manager at Federated Investors. "It's just crazy."
A 449-point selloff took the Dow Jones Industrial Average to its lowest level in almost three years, leaving it
23% below where it stood a year ago. Volume on the New York Stock Exchange was the second highest in
history, falling just shy of the record set on Tuesday. The VIX, a widely watched measure of market volatility
that is often referred to as the "fear index," hit its highest level since late 2002.
In Europe, stock markets lost roughly 2% of their value. In Russia, the scene of recent massive declines,
trading on the country's major exchanges was halted for the second day in a row, this time only an hour and a
half into the session. Gold prices rose 9% to $846.60 an ounce amid the global turmoil. In early trading
Thursday, Tokyo stocks were down 3.2%, among other declining markets in the region.
"Forget about retail investors, all the pros are scared," says one broker. "People have no idea where to put
their money."
For now, "if you have cash, you're going to put it in the short-term, most liquid stuff you can," says Steve Van
Order, fixed-income strategist for Calvert Asset Management.
Adding to the fear was a loss in a prominent money-market fund, the Reserve Primary Fund, which held
Lehman Brothers debt. It was the first time since 1994 that such a fund, which is supposed to be as safe as a
bank account, had lost money. The loss was made worse by a run on the fund. Over two days, investors
pulled more than half of their assets from the fund, once valued at $64 billion.
"This is a panic situation" in the bond markets, says Charles Comiskey, head of U.S. government-bond
trading in New York at HSBC Securities USA Inc.
Riskier assets were sold off. Yields on bonds issued by financial companies hit a record high of about six
percentage points above U.S. Treasurys. In the market for credit-default swaps -- essentially insurance
against default on assets tied to corporate debt and mortgage securities -- fears increased on Wednesday
about whether counterparties would be able to honor their agreements. Investors tried to reduce their
exposures to two more big players in the market, Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and Morgan Stanley. That sent
the cost of protection on both Wall Street firms soaring to new highs.

--- Page 123 ---
Page 123 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In the stock market, the pressure on financial firms continued, with Morgan Stanley stock dropping 24% and
Goldman Sachs shares losing 14%.
Investors say the government takeover of AIG and Lehman's bankruptcy filing are evidence that the situation
is grimmer than all but the most pessimistic had expected. Problems have spread from complex debt markets
tied directly to the housing market into plain-vanilla corporate bonds.
"Another front is opening," says Ajay Rajadhyaksha, head of fixed-income research at Barclays Capital.
Some people fear that the dwindling ranks of investment banks, coming at a time when commercial banks are
pulling back on their own use of capital, will prolong the credit crunch.
"It's unclear who is going to be a credit provider going forward, and if having fewer credit providers means
higher costs of borrowing going forward," says Basil Williams, chief executive of hedge-fund manager
Concordia Advisors.
Ordinarily, bondholders are better protected from losses than stock investors. But the events of the past two
weeks have shown that they are vulnerable, too. The Federal Reserve's rescue of AIG doesn't protect the
company's bondholders. That's because the deal, which consists of a high-priced loan to the company from
the government, requires AIG to pay the Treasury before current bondholders. If AIG can't raise enough cash
by selling assets, bondholders won't be fully repaid.
As a result, despite the Fed lifeline, some AIG debt is changing hands at just 40 cents on the dollar, less than
half of the price one week ago. Now that Lehman has defaulted on its debt, its senior bonds are worth as little
as 17 cents on the dollar, traders say.
That's spilled over to other financial names seen as under stress. Bonds of Morgan Stanley are trading at
around 60 cents on the dollar. Goldman Sachs's bonds are trading at prices in the range of 70 cents on the
dollar.
As bond prices dropped, their yields rose. The spread between yields on corporate bonds and safe U.S.
Treasurys have blown out to the widest levels traders have seen in years. On Wednesday, yields on
investment-grade corporate bonds were more than four percentage points higher than comparable Treasury
bonds, according to Merrill Lynch. Junk bonds ended the day more than nine percentage points over
Treasurys, approaching the 2002 high of 10.6 percentage points, according to Merrill.
Short-term debt markets, where companies borrow overnight or in periods up to one year, have dried up. The
money-market-fund managers who normally buy such short-term debt have suffered losses on their holdings
of debt in Lehman Brothers and other financial institutions.
If companies can't borrow in the short-term debt markets, they may be forced to draw down on their revolving
credit lines, yet another drain on banks' dwindling capital.
The Lehman bankruptcy also pressured the market for leveraged loans, which are used by private-equity
firms to finance buyouts. When the firm attempted to sell some of its loan holdings earlier this week, prices
dropped toward 85 cents on the dollar, according to Standard &Poor's Leveraged Commentary &Data.
The damage has gone beyond banks and brokerages. Ford Motor Credit Co., the finance arm of Ford Motor
Co., paid 7.5% for Tuesday-night overnight borrowings, says one trader. Typically, the rate for such debt
would be about one-quarter percentage point over the federal-funds rate, which is currently 2%, he says.
Even for companies considered of the safest credit quality, the cost of overnight debt is rising. General
Electric Co. was forced to pay 3.5% for overnight borrowing on Wednesday, the trader says. In normal times,
GE, which has the highest debt rating, would have to pay the equivalent of the federal-funds rate.
"There's no evident catalyst for ending the pain," says Guy Lebas, chief fixed-income strategist at Janney
Montgomery in Philadelphia.
---
Min Zeng contributed to this article.

The Fed and AIG
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
The Fed and AIG
936 words
18 September 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A24
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
If U.S. officials thought that nationalizing giant insurer AIG would stop the financial panic, markets gave them
a rude reply yesterday. Stocks fell sharply, gold rose $89 (!) an ounce, and spreads on Morgan Stanley and
Goldman Sachs debt widened to canyons over Treasurys. Investors are wondering who's next on the
Treasury-Federal Reserve list for a force majeure takeover, and are selling off accordingly.
Perhaps Secretary Hank Paulson was right that AIG had to be rescued to avoid a broader financial collapse.
We aren't privy to what he and the New York Fed were hearing about AIG's credit default swaps or its
insurance "wraps" for the commercial paper market; maybe unraveling those would have smashed the
corporate debt market or caused a run on money-market accounts. So maybe he had no choice but to rescue
the part of AIG that was a hedge fund wrapped around the world's largest insurer.
But it's precisely this opacity that is the problem for market psychology: These serial nationalizations are
being done out of Mr. Paulson's hip pocket, based on his judgment of what constitutes a systemic risk (Bear
Stearns, Fannie Mae, AIG) and what doesn't (Lehman). There's no transparency, and when Treasury decides
to act it swoops in with a no-bid transaction that dictates terms to the target company. At the very least a
Treasury Secretary should try to justify the nature of such risk when he puts $85 billion in taxpayer cash on
the line.
All the more so if the takeovers inspire even more market fear. Three month T-bill prices plunged at one point
yesterday nearly to 0%, which means investors are willing to accept essentially no return to get a safe harbor.
Gold's leap of more than 11% is also a flight to safety.
The danger is that we will get these financial melodramas every week, if not more frequently. Each one only
frightens the public more and extends the panic. The two surviving big investment banks, Morgan Stanley
and Goldman, continue to operate with enormous leverage yet profess to have enough capital to survive.
That's also what Lehman and AIG thought. Markets are also punishing Washington Mutual, the big savings
bank, and Wachovia, the regional bank, with others to follow if housing prices keep falling.
Sooner rather than later, the Fed is going to run out of money to pull off these government takeovers. Its
balance sheet was designed to finance open-market operations, plus serve as the occasional lender of last
resort for regulated banks. Its assets have long been mainly Treasuries or currency.
Since last December, however, the Fed has made creative use of its discount window with the result that its
balance sheet looks uglier all the time. The Fed has guaranteed $29 billion in dodgy Bear Stearns paper,
opened its window to ever more colorful collateral, and as of Monday even agreed to accept equity. With its
AIG stake, the Fed now owns an insurance company. By our calculations, the Fed has committed some $380
billion of its $888 billion in assets to these mortgage rescue operations. That's nearly half. And yesterday the
Treasury announced it will issue new debt to lend to the Fed, not merely to fund government operations.
These are all taxpayer obligations, and as such they pull the Fed ever more deeply into political decisions
that compromise its independence. The Fed has been pushed into that situation because Treasury lacks the
legal authority for such takeovers (except in the case of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac), while the Fed could
act under section 13(3) of the Federal Reserve Act. But this is unsustainable -- and dangerous.
The first recourse going forward is for Treasury to help Morgan Stanley and other endangered institutions find
new private capital, or, better, private merger partners. Merrill Lynch took itself off the run list by selling to
Bank of America. More than a few Merrill shareholders griped about the price, but they did much better than
AIG holders will with their 80% dilution to Uncle Sam.
If that fails, then the best and perhaps only recourse is to create an entity on the order of a new Resolution
Trust Corporation. Such an agency could become a buyer at a fair and transparent price for distressed debt,

--- Page 125 ---
Page 125 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
as well as the workout home for institutions like AIG that failed because of accounting rules and bad subprime
debt but retain great underlying value.
The details are crucial to making this work right -- to avoid political meddling, for example, and to make sure it
has an end date. But until home prices stabilize, it may be the only way to stop the panic and serial
nationalizations.
---
We're told Treasury has a proposal ready to send to Congress, but that the Members have told Mr. Paulson
they don't want to see it until after Election Day. Mr. Paulson fears that if he does call for action and Congress
refuses, then the contagion would be even worse. Well, how much worse can it get than a failure or two a
week of a major financial institution? The sooner a resolution agency is up and running, the fewer banks will
fail and the lower the ultimate cost to the taxpayer.
Mr. Paulson ought to tell Congress that this authority is essential to stopping a panic, and that the need is
urgent. If Harry Reid and Nancy Pelosi say they can't do it until December or later, then they can take
responsibility for the nationalizations to come.

VeraSun, LaBranche, PolyOne Lead the Way Down
Small Stock Focus
VeraSun, LaBranche, PolyOne Lead the Way Down
By Geoffrey Rogow
829 words
18 September 2008
The Wall Street Journal

C6
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Investors seemingly closed the book on any possibility of a lasting small-cap rally Wednesday as fears about
the future of Wall Street pushed such companies well into the red.
Throughout July and August, small-caps were surging. A rallying dollar and concern about a falling global
economy pushed investors into the domestically focused smaller names.
And while on the outside, Lehman Brothers Holdings's bankruptcy filing, American International Group's
bailout and fears of a domino effect for several other large financial institutions seemed to be isolated just to
Wall Street, few traders are acting that way.
For one, these financial institutions remain the backbone of companies large and small. Their failure
threatens not only companies' ability to develop and innovate, but also destroys some investors' hopes that
deal activity could spur further market growth for smaller names.
"It's going to be a snowball effect. Companies that were doing well aren't going to spend as much. Look at
restaurants and hotels. Wall Street employees alone will hurt that," said Dave Rovelli, managing director of
U.S. equity trading for Canaccord Adams.
At its core, the push on Wall Street has led investors to shun any risk. And, as people rein in risk, small-caps
have gotten crushed.
For Wednesday, the Russell 2000 index of small-capitalization stocks lost 34.27 points, or 4.82%, to 676.38,
and the Standard &Poor's SmallCap 600 declined 15.57, or 4.12%, to 362.07.
After the two-month rally earlier this year, the Russell had pared its losses so dramatically that it was off less
than 1% for the year-to-date as recently as early June. But after a 10% decline over the past month, the index
is now down 12% for the year.
Financials were among the weakest performers, including a decline of 48 cents, or 10%, to $4.21, for
LaBranche, on the New York Stock Exchange.
Industrials, which are heavily tied to the domestic economy, fell rapidly in Wednesday's session. Among the
big decliners, Standex International (NYSE) fell 2.15, or 7.6%, to 26.24.
Consumer stocks were led down by closeout retailer Tuesday Morning, which closed down 42 cents, or 8.4%,
at 4.58.
Still, despite the selloff, some portfolio managers say they are remaining above water. Michael Petroff, a vice
president and portfolio manager of the Heartland Value Plus Fund, notes his fund fell only 1% Wednesday
and remains up 11% for the year while the market crumbles.
"We have a maniacal focus on levered companies, which has served us well," said Mr. Petroff, who notes the
fund also targets roughly 90% of the portfolio at companies that pay dividends.
In the equities market, Mr. Petroff and other money managers note this focus on finding the least-levered
operations in each sector should serve investors well.
And, at the same time, be ready to play on a short-term countertrend. The recent Hurricane Ike that hit the
Gulf Coast is a perfect example. Shares of some refiners surged, while engines maker Briggs &Stratton has
now tacked on 8.6% in a week. For Wednesday, Briggs &Stratton tacked on 37 cents, or 2.1%, to 17.63.

sales forecasts Wednesday.

forecast for second-half sales growth, citing lower demand in Europe, a weaker euro and "more challenging
than expected" demand and pricing trends in the North American housing and auto markets.

Gold Rises to $846.60 As 'Ultimate' Haven
Commodities Report
Gold Rises to $846.60 As 'Ultimate' Haven
By Matt Whittaker
819 words
18 September 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
The name of American International Group Inc. was incorrectly given as American Investment Group Inc. in
Thursday's Commodities Report.
(WSJ September 19, 2008)

Gold futures are now within striking distance of $900 after the metal's largest-ever single-day dollar gain
reversed recent bearish momentum as speculators and other investors fled the financial sector in search of
safety.
Thinly traded nearby September gold on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange surged
$70.10, or 9%, to $846.60 an ounce.
This was the biggest dollar gain for a front-month contract since as far back as daily history is available from
the exchange -- January 1975. It marked the largest percentage gain in nine years.
The most-active December contract continued posting gains in after-hours electronic trading, peaking at
$872.90 after settling at $850.50.
Gold benefited mightily Wednesday on fear of systemic risk highlighted by the American Investment Group
Inc. bailout, said Stephen Platt, an analyst with Archer Financial Services. "The market is certainly fearful of
that systemic risk moving into the global market," he said.
Earlier in the week, gold had seen some tepid flight-to-quality buying, but to the surprise of many observers it
couldn't push past $800. That is because investors also were fleeing to Treasurys and the U.S. dollar for
refuge as Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc. filed for bankruptcy-court protection and Merrill Lynch &Co. agreed
to be acquired by Bank of America Corp.
That changed for gold Wednesday, as the increasing tempo of dire financial news left investors fleeing
equities and clamoring for more safe-haven options, in essence a delayed reaction for gold as the
quintessential refuge in times of market crisis.
The main cause of worry came as the U.S. government took control of AIG with an $85 billion bailout that
gives it a 79.9% stake in one of the world's biggest insurers.
Kitco Bullion Dealers analyst Jon Nadler in a research note likened Wednesday's move in gold to its January
1980 price increase amid a confluence of factors.
The worsening financial-sector news has shifted the momentum for gold, said Carlos Sanchez,
precious-metals analyst with CPM Group.
Just last week, large speculators were aggressively liquidating bullish positions and adding bearish ones in
gold, sending the metal down more than 10% in four days.
That is reversing as funds and proprietary bank-trading desks are now putting in fresh long positions, Mr.
Sanchez said. Longs are expectations that prices will rise.
"Momentum is to the upside," Mr. Sanchez said. "I think the overall trend is higher. It's the ultimate safe
haven; it's a currency that's lasted through the ages."

--- Page 129 ---
Page 129 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
If follow-through momentum buying continues, gold could be looking at $900 before the month is out, said
Ralph Preston, senior market analyst with Heritage West Financial.
There may be profit-taking for the next session or two, but the $900-an-ounce gold benchmark -- which will
offer resistance at first -- is certainly attainable this month, said Mr. Sanchez.
Wednesday's safe-haven rally also was seen as a continuation of seemingly incomplete gains Monday,
following the Lehman and Merrill news as AIG searched for cash.
Given gold's previous losses, some observers were surprised that the metal's safe-haven gains at the
beginning of the week weren't much stronger -- the December contract peaked at $791.40 Monday -- at least
carrying the metal above $800.
It "wasn't that good a performance" as safe-haven money also was going into Treasurys, which in turn was
supporting the U.S. dollar, said Dave Rinehimer, director of Citi Futures Perspective at Citigroup Global
Markets.
But Wednesday's moves lower in the dollar and higher oil prices are paving the way for increased
safe-haven interest in gold, Mr. Rinehimer said. The metal's decisive move over the technical and
psychological benchmark $800 level is stimulating additional buying in the metal.
"Considering gold's come down so far from where it was, today you're starting to see some buying interest,"
Mr. Rinehimer said.

Commodities Report

17 September 2008
The Wall Street Journal

C2
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold seems to be losing its luster as a safe haven.
As the financial tsunami hit Wall Street on Monday, gold added just 3% in a flight to safety. On Tuesday, it
fell $6.60, or 0.8%, to $776.50 a troy ounce -- a dull performance, considering the magnitude of the market
turmoil.
Analysts say the yellow metal is in for more bumps in the road ahead as financial institutions scramble to
shore up capital to stay alive. Increased volatility is expected to last at least for the next few days, as the
woes at Lehman Brothers, Merrill Lynch and AIG have triggered a new round of risk reduction across all
financial markets.
Commodities are particularly vulnerable to a selloff as investors unwind winning positions after years of a
run-up.
So far this year, gold has taken the wildest ride since 1980, with a swing of nearly $300 a troy ounce. A
straight-up move took gold through the $1,000 mark in March, before it wobbled around $900 for months.
Since July, commodities have suffered from a broad washout amid the dollar's sudden strength and global
economic weakness, sending gold into a tailspin. It is down 6.4% this month and 7% this year.
Gold's role as a safe haven has been "overwhelmed by the massive storm-surge flight to cash and the
indiscriminate selling of securities and commodities," Jeffrey Nichols, managing director of American Precious
Metals Advisors, wrote in a report Monday. Since mid-July's lows for stocks, investors have been pulling
money out of commodities funds, according to EPFR Global, which tracks asset-allocation data.
Nearly 40% of the demand for gold goes into investments such as exchange-traded funds, coins and bars,
while the rest goes to applications such as jewelry, dental and electronics and is vulnerable to economic
weakness. Prices for other commodities, such as crude oil, generally are based more on supply and demand.
Gold "naturally attracted more investment interest than other commodities, and now [this money] is exiting
the market quickly," said James Steel, chief commodity analyst at HSBC.
As investors in SPDR Gold Trust, the largest gold ETF, redeemed shares, the fund has dumped a total of
91.24 metric tons of gold onto the market since July -- some 5% of the world's annual gold production.
Some investors are betting that gold prices will fall further. Direxion Commodity Trends Strategy Fund, a $50
million commodity mutual fund, had been buying gold until early August. It now allocates nearly 12% of its
assets to short-selling gold futures.
And 2100 Xenon Group LLC, a Chicago-based commodity hedge fund overseeing $115 million in assets,
also has been shorting gold futures since July, said Jay R. Feuerstein, the fund's chief investment officer.
"Shorting is the best risk-managing strategy now," he said.
But some gold bugs are still bullish. Stocks are reeling and the credit mess hasn't been cleaned up, while
inflationary and recessionary concerns continue to take a toll on consumers.
First Eagle Gold Fund, a $1.1 billion mutual fund, was down 11.5% in August, but portfolio manager
Jean-Marie Eveillard said he is not selling gold assets, now 27.3% in bullion, with the rest in gold mining
stocks. He predicts that the federal government will have to inject more money into the financial system,
which will lead to inflation and push gold prices back up.

The Fed's Epic Day
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
The Fed's Epic Day
353 words
17 September 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Federal Reserve had one of the more momentous days in its history yesterday, holding firm on interest
rates at midday and then deciding to lend $85 billion to troubled insurer AIG late in the evening. The AIG
decision has manifold implications and will take more time to assess, but the other call we readily endorse.
Our view is that 90% of central banking is about character. Can a Fed chairman stand up to the inevitable
lobbying from business and the political class when the economic moment demands a hard line for hard
money? Any central banker can say yes to easier money. The great ones can also say no.
Yesterday was a moment for saying no, given how far the Fed had cut rates in the past year and the inflation
spike that came in its wake. While the price of commodities in general and gold have fallen sharply in recent
weeks, they are coming down from epic highs. Oil at $92 a barrel is still some $20 higher than it was a year
ago, despite falling demand due to a slowing world economy. And despite yesterday's report that consumer
prices fell 0.1% in August, largely due to oil's decline, prices are still up 5.4% over the last 12 months. The
decline in oil and the rise in the dollar in recent weeks will help the overall economy far more than any further
reduction in interest rates. As energy and food prices fall, the result will be an income transfer to
middle-class consumers who have been shell-shocked by rising prices this year.
Meanwhile, the details of the Fed's AIG decision will have to be inspected, not least for what it does to the
Fed's own balance sheet. We've been worried about the Fed's willingness to take on increasingly shaky
assets as collateral. The decision to let Lehman Brothers fail but to rescue AIG in the same week could also
stand some parsing. At least the Fed's interest rate decision put Main Street ahead of Wall Street.

was a record, with 9.31 billion trading.
"This is a fast-moving market with a lot of incomplete information and a lot of rumors. That is a recipe for a
treacherous investing landscape," said Andy Brooks, head of stock trading at Baltimore asset-management
firm T. Rowe Price. "The concept that Merrill Lynch could merge with Bank of America in a 24-hour period is
just unbelievable. There are enormous things happening, and it is going to require enormous effort to get
things settled down a little bit."
Just after the opening bell, the Dow was down 174.81 points, meaning the swing that followed totaled more
than 316 points. AIG was down 74% to start the day. That once-proud company climbed briefly back into
positive territory near day's end, only to finish down 21% to $3.75, a 23-year low close. More than a billion
AIG shares changed hands, a record and nearly 30 times AIG's average for 2008.
Reflecting the uncertainty, AIG shares were down nearly 46% in after-hours trading following a report that the
firm might be placed in some form of conservatorship. Later in the night, news emerged that the government
had agreed to an emergency rescue of AIG.
The dollar rose on the Fed's decision to hold interest rates steady, because steady rates mean returns from
dollar-based money markets and bonds don't fall. Meanwhile, a report showed consumer prices falling in
August. Oil, gasoline, copper, gold and silver futures all fell on the day, further easing inflation fears and
fueling hopes that the burden on consumers might ease.
The 10-year Treasury note, whose price rose early in the day as investors sought its relative safety, finished
slightly down as investors moved money back to the greater risk of the stock market.
Brokerage firm Morgan Stanley announced quarterly results after the market close, sooner than expected,
and they weren't as bad as feared. Its stock was down 1.8% in after-hours trading.
Plenty of signs of anxiety remained. Despite a positive finish for all the major indexes, more stocks fell than
rose on the New York Stock Exchange.
The Vix, a measure of market fear based on options trading and tracked by the Chicago Board Options
Exchange, fell from Monday's level, but still finished above 30. It has surpassed that level only rarely this
year, during times of particular anxiety.
Of particular concern to many investors, credit markets indicated that investor fear has abated only a little.
Although they recovered a bit during the afternoon, so-called spreads between the interest rates paid by
corporate borrowers and by the government remained much wider than normal, reflecting continuing fears of
corporate defaults.

--- Page 134 ---
Page 134 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The high spreads meant there would have been little more than psychological value to borrowers from a Fed
rate-cut, analysts said, since Fed rates don't directly affect spreads. Some investors applauded the Fed's
refusal to bow to market pressures and cut its rates.
"The stresses have been so intense in credit markets that the only thing the Fed can really do here is make
sure that markets are functioning and clearing," said Michael Darda, chief economist at brokerage firm MKM
Partners in Greenwich, Conn. The Fed injected $70 billion into credit markets Monday and the same amount
again on Tuesday in an effort to keep them fluid.

Oil, Gold Take Divergent Paths, Highlighting Commodities Jam --- Steady Dollar Was Wild Card for Both; More Pain in Store?
Commodities Report
Oil, Gold Take Divergent Paths, Highlighting Commodities Jam --- Steady Dollar Was Wild Card for
Both; More Pain in Store?
By Gregory Meyer and Matt Whittaker
763 words
16 September 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C7
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Oil, which once flirted with safe-haven status, has come back to earth.
Gold, meanwhile, bucked the broader commodities correction and rose Monday as Wall Street convulsed.
The wild card for both was a steady dollar, which has contributed to oil's late-summer selloff from July's
all-time record highs and capped gold's gains.
The disjointed moves by oil and gold, which until recently were two popular bets investors used to hedge
against weak dollar and rising inflation expectations, underscores how quickly negative sentiment has
pervaded commodities. Earlier this year, as oil surpassed $100 a barrel and as gold breached $1,000 an
ounce, bullish momentum in the two fed off each other and spilled over into other commodities. Now, as fears
of a world-wide economic slowdown are heightened by the radical retooling of the largest U.S. banks,
supply-and-demand fundamentals that had powered commodities' rise are adding to the weakness.
Benchmark crude oil futures settled below $100 for the first time since March, falling 5.4% to end at $95.71 a
barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Nearby gold futures rose almost 3% to settle at $783.10 an
ounce on the Comex division of the Nymex.
"While the argument is made that oil is among the safe-haven investments in this most troubled of times, it
doesn't make the cut," said John Kilduff, senior vice president at brokerage MF Global Ltd. in New York.
"Only gold and U.S. Treasurys make that cut."
The U.S. financial sector, under strain from an extended credit crunch, finally snapped over the weekend.
Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc. filed for bankruptcy protection, Merrill Lynch rushed into a merger with Bank
of America Corp. and insurance giant American International Group Inc. scrambled for capital.
"Gold forums were electrified overnight and expectations were for an opening where gold was up from $50 to
$100," Kitco Bullion Dealers analyst John Nadler said in a research note. However, "gold reached a high . . .
on the developments and then it gave up those gains as it continued to obsess about caving crude oil values
instead."
The fact that gold wasn't able to bounce higher, given the magnitude of the weekend's financial news,
doesn't bode well for the metal.
"The financial developments over the weekend clearly have spooked the market, and people worry that the
global economy continues to be under a lot of stress," pressuring petroleum demand, said Adam Sieminski,
chief energy economist at Deutsche Bank.
High oil prices have pressured equities markets, as higher energy prices have sapped U.S. consumers'
spending power, but any hopes for a rally on lower oil prices were quashed by financial-sector woes.
With unemployment rising, lower energy prices will offer relief to consumers when credit is hard to come by
and gasoline is more expensive than it was last year. Although oil futures have plummeted, retail gasoline
prices in the U.S. are surging due to spot shortages caused by Hurricane Ike, which made landfall on the Gulf
Coast on Saturday.
Some traders also wonder how the selloff in crude will affect banks like Goldman Sachs Group Inc., which
have held up well through the credit crisis partly due to bets on commodities.
Lehman had a commodities footprint, albeit not a very big one. Lehman's own "value at risk" from
commodities, or potential one-day losses from trading positions, averaged $15 million in the three months

--- Page 136 ---
Page 136 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
ended Aug. 31 -- less than half the most recently reported exposures at commodity powerhouses Morgan
Stanley and Goldman Sachs. Analysts said rival banks were scrambling to take on the bank's commodities
clients.
Lehman was suspended from trading on ICE Futures Europe after the exchange's clearing house declared
the company a defaulter. CME Group Inc. said it facilitated the transfer of derivatives positions from clients of
Lehman to other entities.
If the fund selling in commodities continues, both gold and oil could move further south. Further pressure
may be coming due to the sharp selloff in global stock markets Monday, because participants may have been
selling some gold to meet margin calls from creditors, said Andrew Montano, director of precious metals at
Scotia Mocatta.
Other commodity market such as sugar, coffee and copper were also lower following the financial jitters but
pared losses near the end of the day.
---
Rob Curran and Doug Cameron contributed to this article.

Large Stock Focus: AIG Plunges 61%; Merrill Adds a Penny --- Bank of America Falls 21% as Selling Hits Other Sectors
Large Stock Focus: AIG Plunges 61%; Merrill Adds a Penny --- Bank of America Falls 21% as Selling
Hits Other Sectors
By Rob Curran
680 words
16 September 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
In a post-Lehman world, no news is bad news: A lack of progress for American International Group in its
effort to raise capital precipitated a 60% plunge for its stock during Monday's overall market rout.
Shares of AIG, the giant insurer, fell $7.38, or 61%, to $4.76 as investors worried that its exposure to
mortgage-linked derivatives would make it the next domino to fall in the credit crisis. The drop for AIG, a
member of the Dow Jones Industrial Average, contributed to the 504.48-points, or 4.4%, slide in the Dow to
10917.51, its lowest close since July 2006.
Similarly, the broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index dropped through the 1200 level, thought to be key
support, falling 59 points, or 4.7%, to 1192.70. The Nasdaq Composite fell 81.36 points, or 3.6%, to 2179.91,
its lowest close since March 17 of this year.
Lehman Brothers Holdings fell 3.44, or 94%, to 21 cents. The Wall Street firm seemed to be ending its 158
years in operation with a bankruptcy-court filing after last-minute rescue talks failed over the weekend.
"There's no question we're having a domino effect," said Art Cashin, head of UBS floor operations on the
NYSE. "That's probably the reason the Fed was so interested in a shotgun marriage for Merrill Lynch . . .
They were probably the next target" for the bears.
One domino plucked out of the line, Merrill Lynch ended the session a penny higher at 17.06. Shares had
risen as much as 33% after Bank of America struck a $50 billion buyout deal for the brokerage. After the
demise of Lehman Brothers, Merrill faced the unenviable prospect of being the smallest of the independent
brokers on Wall Street.
The price of the deal is linked to that of Bank of America's shares, which fell 7.19, or 21%, to 26.55, and was
the second-biggest decliner.
Now only two of the independent Wall Street titans are left standing, and they will soon inform investors of
their positions in earnings reports, beginning Tuesday with Goldman Sachs Group. Goldman Sachs declined
18.71, or 12%, to 135.50. Morgan Stanley fell 5.04, or 14%, to 32.19.
Shares of Washington Mutual, a mortgage lender with exposure in California and other housing markets
where the first signs of trouble emerged, fell 73 cents, or 27%, to 2, a drop of 96% from its peak.
Wachovia, another bank weighed down by mortgage exposure, fell 3.56, or 25%, to 10.71.
The selling extended beyond the financial sector, however, as traders observed a phenomenon known as
"deleveraging." That means financial institutions were selling assets and commodities in order to pay back
debt and reduce risk. The price of gold and Treasurys rallied, indicating investors were running for cover in
the most secure investments.
And among the stronger stocks on the Dow were "widows-and-orphans" stocks like Coca-Cola, which tacked
on 25 cents to 54.75; McDonald's, which fell 34 cents to 63.72; and Procter &Gamble, down 1.01, or 1.4%, to
72.14.
Among other consumer staples, Longs Drug Stores rose 4.09, or 5.7%, to 75.75 after Walgreen made a
surprise offer to pry the drugstore chain from rival suitor CVS Caremark by offering a higher price.
As oil prices fell to their lowest finish since February, the energy sector was the second weakest of the 10
subsectors in the Standard &Poor's 500.

--- Page 138 ---
Page 138 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Shares of Sunoco fell 9.67, or 20%, to 38.18, as Hurricane Ike wreaked less damage than feared on
refineries and the national gasoline supply.
Even General Motors, which usually benefits from drops in oil prices, declined 1.57, or 12%, to 11.44.
Trading volume on the NYSE Composite was the heaviest in history, with 8.14 billion shares trading.

Mining-Stock Fall Opens Door --- Decline Could Lead To Bigger Role For Private Equity
Commodities Report
Mining-Stock Fall Opens Door --- Decline Could Lead To Bigger Role For Private Equity
By Matthew Walls
610 words
15 September 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
LONDON -- The downturn in mining stocks may open the door for private-equity funds to play a bigger role in
the industry.
Their entry could ease a logjam of underfunded projects due to the credit crunch that some industry
participants believe may keep metal supplies tighter than expected.
Many private-equity funds avoided miners during the recent boom. Valuations were too high to provide the
returns they sought, while booming demand from institutional and individual, or "retail," investors for mining
shares meant smaller companies could easily obtain capital for their projects through stock sales.
"We weren't doing a lot of investments at the peak of the market," said Jon Dugdale, president of Asian Lion
Ltd., a $21.6 million fund established by Australian mining-investment firm Lion Selection Ltd. to take stakes
in Asian resource projects.
The fund has invested just $4.5 million since its 2006 launch.
But now, smaller exploration and mining companies with projects in development are grappling with the credit
crunch and lower metals prices that have helped send share prices tumbling.
Many might see private-equity financing as preferable to traditional debt deals and their high interest rates.
MinQuest, a 225 million Canadian dollar ($212 million) fund backed by Caisse de Depot et Placement du
Quebec, one of Canada's largest institutional investors, expects its deal flow to "increase substantially in the
next couple of years," said Paul Carmel, its president and founder.
"With the buoyant prices there's been a lot of capital raised, a lot of projects have been started or discovered.
So there's a pipeline that's full, but now the capital tap has been turned off," he said.
Private-equity funds foresee a shakeout for mining stocks in the coming year, as hedge funds and other
investment groups that had been providing funding withdraw.
"Long-term fundamental institutions such as ourselves may view their current situation as a buying
opportunity," said Peter Ruxton, head of mining investment at private-equity group Actis, which has $150
million invested in African-based mining companies. "It depends on whether you think this is the end of the
commodity bull run or a temporary aberration due to the credit crunch."
Some funds are raising new war chests.
Lion Selection in July closed its $79.2 million African Lion 3 fund, its third Africa-focused mining fund. A
month earlier, Baker Steel Capital Managers LLP launched a $60 million fund to invest in early-stage mining
projects.
Other private-equity firms, including the Sentient Group, Pacific Road Capital Management, Resource Capital
Funds, Black River Asset Management LLC and Emerging Capital Partners, have several hundred million
dollars under management to invest in the mining industry.
Many of these funds are looking to get returns of two times to five times their investment over a seven- to
10-year investment horizon.
One mining company that has turned to private equity is Minerva Resources PLC, a GBP 3.5 million ($6.3
million) U.K. company listed on the London Stock Exchange's Alternative Investment Market.
It is working on a gold project in Ethiopia.

--- Page 140 ---
Page 140 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Some private-equity firms are focusing on existing mining and metal operations. Last year, U.K.-based Klesch
&Co. agreed in principle to purchase a 290,000-metric-ton aluminum smelter in the Netherlands from Alcan,
now owned by Rio Tinto, and is in talks with European steelmaker Corus to buy its two aluminum smelters in
Germany and the Netherlands.
It also is developing an $8 billion greenfield aluminum smelter and oil refinery in Libya.

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
By Mark Gongloff
514 words
8 September 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Deflation Buzz
Drives Talk
Of a Rate Cut
After months of thinking the Fed's next move would be a rate increase, the market is suddenly toying with the
idea of another cut.
Inflation seems to have buckled under the heft of a global economic slowdown. Crude-oil prices are down
27% from their July peak. The dollar has rallied, throwing more dirt on inflation -- though the temporary
government takeover of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac could change that. Even gold, investors' friend when
prices rise, has declined.
Market fears about prices have nearly vanished. The gap between the yield on 10-year Treasury
Inflation-Protected Securities and the 10-year note has shrunk to less than 2%, the lowest since 2003. This
suggests investors see consumer-price inflation averaging less than 2% annualized for the next 10 years,
well within the Fed's comfort zone.
As recently as June, the federal-funds futures market, where traders bet on future Fed moves, priced in three
rate increases by year's end. Now it is pricing in no chance of a rate increase. At times last week the market
began to price in a slim chance of a cut this year.
August's jump in unemployment makes a cut seem more likely. Further labor-market weakness would hurt
the economy, while keeping wage gains in check, preventing the sort of wage-price spiral that let inflation
get out of hand in the 1970s.
A different kind of -flation, deflation, seems to be gaining more currency these days. In a note to clients that
roiled financial markets last week, Pacific Investment Management Co. managing director Bill Gross said
tighter credit standards were forcing homeowners, banks and investors to cut back on debt and sell assets to
raise cash, a trend that is deflating asset prices now and could lead to broader deflation later.
He said prices for U.S. homes, stocks and bonds combined were down 10% from a year ago, a rate
associated with the Great Depression.
Deflation may not sound bad, considering food, gasoline and other prices are still high. Widespread deflation
can delay purchases and investments as people assume prices will keep falling.
It is too early to worry about that. The job market would have to get much weaker, pushing wages lower, to
set off the sort of deflationary alarm bells that rang at the Fed back in 2003, when it slashed its key rate target
to 1%, notes Morgan Stanley economist David<TH>Greenlaw.
Many economists, and some Fed policy makers, think rates are low enough. If the Fannie-Freddie takeover
results in lower mortgage rates, the Fed might have one less reason to lower its target rate.
Still, expectations are shifting. The two-year Treasury note yield is nearly as low as fed funds. Breaking below
it historically signals expectations of a Fed rate cut. The futures market, on the other hand, still expects the
Fed to raise rates in the spring. That may be too soon.
---

Tide Turns As Financials Rebound, Commodity Prices Sink
Tide Turns As Financials Rebound, Commodity Prices Sink
By Neil King Jr., Robert Guy Matthews and Ann Davis
1,501 words
3 September 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Investors' concerns about a slowing global economy are trumping their worries about the troubled financial
system. The result is a reversal of the dynamic that held for much of this year: Commodity prices now are
dropping, while the dollar and stocks of companies hit by the credit crunch are rebounding.
Oil prices fell sharply Tuesday after Hurricane Gustav skipped past major refineries and oil platforms in the
Gulf of Mexico. U.S. benchmark crude sank $5.75 a barrel, or just under 5% from last Friday's close, to
$109.71 on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
It was the latest leg down for oil, which is off nearly $40 a barrel since mid-July, leading some to predict
prices below $100 by year's end. Natural gas fell 68.2 cents per million BTUs from Friday, or 8.59%, to settle
at $7.261.
Many commodities' prices have slid in recent months, and the most recent to show weakness is steel. It's
down as much as 30% in some markets as suppliers cut prices following a first-half surge to a June record.
"Demand is slowing around the world," said Charles Bradford, an analyst at Bradford Securities.
Even as sentiment has turned negative on commodities, worries about the financial system have abated,
despite continued problems on Wall Street and in the housing market. Oil is down about 25% since it peaked
in intraday trading on July 11, but U.S. financial stocks have rebounded more than 16% in the same period.
The dollar has strengthened, most dramatically against the British pound.
Another sign of investors' shifting sentiment: Gold is down 20% from its peak earlier this year above $1,000
an ounce. Gold has flirted with sub-$800 prices, briefly trading below that price on Tuesday. It closed down
2.9% to $805 an ounce.
Stocks rallied strongly at the start of Tuesday's U.S. trading, with the Dow Jones Industrial Average up 246
points at one point. But the market was dragged down in part by falling energy and commodity stocks, which
ended down 5.5% and 2.5%, respectively. The Dow closed off 26.63 points at 11516.92. It is up 5.1% from its
low for the year, which it touched July 15, four days after oil peaked.
The shift is a new reality for global markets. Commodities are softening on any hint of bearish news and
shrugging off most bullish news -- suggesting a significant shift in sentiment away from the sector and leaving
many traders wondering what the floor will be.
But U.S. stocks have rebounded despite a stream of bearish news, including a weakening economy. Besides
financial shares, stocks tied to consumer spending, such as auto makers and retailers, in August had their
biggest one-month gain since 2003.
The decline in commodities has been driven by weakening demand, so it's not necessarily good news for
stocks or economies. In addition, shifts in investor sentiment can come with little warning. Either more bad
news from a big bank that invested in mortgages -- or a sign the global economy isn't slowing as much as
feared -- could reverse the trend.
But for now, lower commodity prices hold potential relief to businesses and consumers either directly or
through lower interest rates, which are kept up by inflation fears. Lower commodity prices ease those fears
somewhat. On Tuesday, the 10-year Treasury note rose 19/32, lowering its yield to 3.746%, its lowest yield
since April 23.
The commodity-price slide is causing worries among producers, in particular the Organization of Petroleum
Exporting Countries, which produces about 40% of the world's oil.
Some members, particularly Saudi Arabia, have raised alarms that costly crude could damage the world
economy and lead to long-term diminished demand for oil. But hawkish members, above all Iran and

--- Page 149 ---
Page 149 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Venezuela, are loath to see oil fall below $100 a barrel and are expected to lead a campaign for OPEC to cut
back on production when the 13 cartel members meet next week in Vienna.
Still unclear is Saudi Arabia's position. It has consistently raised production over the past year, lifting its
output by around a million barrels a day, to 9.7 million barrels. All of OPEC supplied around 32.8 million
barrels daily in August to a world market that is consuming just over 86.6 million barrels a day.
Unless oil prices continue to fall sharply, it appears unlikely that OPEC will announce a formal cut. Just two
months ago, Saudi Arabia held an unusual summit of oil consumers and producers in Jidda to discuss how to
stop what was then the soaring price of oil, which at the time was up more than 60% since the year began. It
would be awkward for Saudi Oil Minister Ali Naimi and others within the cartel to overtly defend any price
above $100 a barrel, analysts and OPEC officials say.
Steel had been one of the few metals whose price stayed near historic highs as prices of other metals
fluctuated and, more recently, trended downward. The prices of copper, aluminum, nickel and scrap have
fallen. But the relentless two-year string of price rises in steel appears over. On Tuesday, ArcelorMittal, the
largest steelmaker, said it would cut prices in South Africa as much as 8% across all its steel products
because of lower international prices. The cut, starting Oct. 1, will be the first in a year.
In Eastern Europe, the price for hot-rolled steel, the basic building block for the majority of steel products, has
fallen about 30% to roughly $1,000 a metric ton in less than two months. In the U.S., prices of domestic
hot-rolled and cold-rolled steel are off about 8%, or $80 a ton, in the past month.
Steel prices in China have been sliding since July, following a first-half surge. In India, top steel producers
recently decided at the end of a three-month price freeze to keep prices steady. In Brazil, prices for imported
plate, used most often in heavy equipment, have fallen by about $200 a metric ton, or some 15%.
Some of the decline is a normal part of summer, when auto makers change models and when building and
road construction in very hot regions, such as the Middle East, takes a pause.
But that can't explain the steep dropoff across the globe, said John Anton, a steel economist for Global
Insight, a financial and information company in Waltham, Mass. He said steel is softening because price
increases have finally hit their limit as the outlooks for the European and U.S. economies look dimmer than
earlier this year.
The price of nickel, used to make stainless steel, has fallen as demand for stainless-steel appliances and
other applications has dropped this year; that has prompted makers of stainless steel, including South
Korea's Posco, to cut prices. Stainless-steel stockpiles have grown in the past few months, according to Peter
Fish, chairman of MEPS International Ltd., a London-based steel research and consulting firm.
Copper dropped 10.70 cents, or 3.1%, to $3.3225 a pound Tuesday on the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange. Aluminum hit a seven-month low on the London Metal Exchange.
In food commodities, wheat, soybeans and corn all slid a few percentage points Tuesday and are well off
their highs this spring and summer. Soybeans and corn are more than 20% below their 2008 peaks and
wheat is more than 40% off.
Freight markets aren't signaling optimism for a commodity rebound. Expectations for bustling fourth-quarter
shipping are starting to come down, said Mike Reardon, a vice president at Imarex Inc., which operates an
electronic and telephone exchange for freight-rate futures.
Raw-materials demand has sent freight rates on a volatile course over the past few years. The Chinese have
been willing to pay high freight rates for iron ore to make steel, for example. Buyers were willing to hire large
ships to carry iron ore for as much as $215,000 a day as recently as mid-May, Mr. Reardon says. A widely
watched blended freight-rate is now down to $105,000.
Mr. Reardon said that Chinese buyers aren't importing as much iron ore for steel mills as expected, and that
iron-ore stockpiles in China grew in the past few weeks.
The oil markets have seen an even steeper dropoff in freight rates, an indication that buyers are no longer
importing as much. Mr. Reardon said rates for very large crude carriers recently plunged to as low as $10,000
a day from a July high of about $200,000 on certain routes. Rates have recovered in the past few weeks to
about $50,000 because some ship owners refused voyages that would lose money.

Oil Drives Up European Markets --- Airlines and Banks Lead the Gainers; Rio Tinto Falls 3.6%
World Stock Markets
Oil Drives Up European Markets --- Airlines and Banks Lead the Gainers; Rio Tinto Falls 3.6%
By Sarah Turner
693 words
3 September 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
LONDON -- A big drop in crude-oil futures helped European airlines and banks climb strongly Tuesday,
offsetting weakness for commodity-sector companies.
Shares of British Airways, Carnival Corp. and many other companies rallied in London, while moves by the
government to support the housing market also shored up sentiment. British Airways rose 4.4%, cruise
operator Carnival climbed 7.4%, while tour operator Thomas Cook Group jumped 7.3%.
The pan-European Dow Jones Stoxx 600 index gained 1% to 290.08, its fifth gain in six sessions, as crude-oil
futures continued Monday's slide.
The latest retreat in oil futures, on milder-than-expected damage in the Gulf of Mexico from Hurricane Gustav,
is positive for stocks as it suggests that the underlying trend for oil prices is down, said Bernard McAlinden,
strategist at NCB Stockbrokers in Dublin.
"Lower oil prices are a force for lower inflation which becomes a case for lower interest rates sooner rather
than later," he said.
Big users of fuel such as airlines advanced. Ryanair Holdings climbed 8.7% in Dublin and rival budget carrier
easyJet jumped 11% in London.
Rate-sensitive banks and construction stocks gained as well. Building-materials group Saint Gobain firmed
5.5% in Paris and Credit Suisse rose 1.9% in Zurich. The insurer Swiss Re advanced 2.8% in Zurich, aided
by limited damage by Gustav.
In FRANKFURT, shares of Deutsche Boerse jumped 6.6% as U.S. hedge fund Atticus and activist investor
the Children's Investment Fund teamed up to demand changes at the stock-exchange operator, including a
sale of one unit. Infineon Technologies climbed 5.9% amid renewed speculation that Micron Technology
might buy its majority-owned memory chip maker Qimonda. The German DAX 30 index climbed 1.5% to
6518.47.
In LONDON, the drop in oil prices pushed down oil producers. BP fell 1.8% and Tullow Oil dropped 5.1%.
Miner Rio Tinto fell 3.6% as gold futures plummeted. The commodity-heavy U.K. FTSE 100 index rose 0.3%
to 5620.70.
In PARIS, the French CAC-40 index gained 1.5% to 4539.07, its highest close since June 19.
Thailand Drops 2.3%
Amid Political Crisis
Thai stocks fell as the government imposed a state of emergency in the capital. Markets elsewhere in Asia
took much of their direction from falling oil prices.
The Stock Exchange of Thailand Index dropped 2.3% to 659.51, its lowest close since Feb. 1, 2007, when it
hit 657. The index has fallen 23% this year.
"It seems like there's no easy way out of the ongoing political crisis, and the tension is likely to linger.
Therefore, the stock market is bound to keep drifting lower," said Bualuang Securities strategist Pongrat
Ratanatawanananda.
Foreign investors trimmed holdings, fearing the political upheaval will hurt the economy and corporate
earnings. "Foreign investors just don't think twice before hurrying to cash out after seeing these headlines,"

--- Page 152 ---
Page 152 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
said Kim Eng Securities strategist Mayuree Chowvikran. Shipping stocks fell on fears trade will be disrupted
by port strikes. Thoresen Thai Agencies sank 8.7%.
In TOKYO, the Nikkei Stock Average fell 1.8% to 12609.47 as falling oil prices dragged down commodity
stocks. The index initially rose, despite concerns that Prime Minister Yasuo Fukuda's decision to resign could
hurt sentiment. Inpex Holdings skidded 6.7%, while trading house Marubeni slid 4.9%.
In MUMBAI, India's 30-stock Sensitive Index climbed 3.8% to 15049.86 amid hopes the drop in oil prices
could ease inflation pressures and lower interest rates. ICICI Bank rose 7.3%.
In HONG KONG, the Hang Seng Index rose 0.7% to 21042.46. Cnooc fell 5.2% on weaker oil prices. But
lower fuel prices boosted Air China 8.7%.
In SEOUL, the Kospi extended its fall as the won continued to weaken and investors feared an exodus of
foreign capital. The index shed 0.5% to 1407.14, its lowest close in nearly 18 months. It plunged 4.1% on
Monday.

International Finance: India Launches Futures In Rupees, With Limits
International Finance: India Launches Futures In Rupees, With Limits
By Subhadip Sircar
524 words
29 August 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MUMBAI -- India's National Stock Exchange on Friday kicks off futures trading in the rupee, a small but
important step for Indian markets that have been starved of new products for years.
A cautious central bank has kept the rules on the contract tight to avoid a stampede of offshore speculators
that could disrupt the market. That could mean a slow start for the new contract.
But the new currency futures contract will help address the needs of a growing class of small and midsize
companies in India as well as individuals who want to hedge their exposure to the dollar-rupee exchange
rate.
"It is about creating an incremental liquidity pool," said Hemant Mishr, managing director and regional head of
corporate sales for South Asia at Standard Chartered Bank in Mumbai.
Mr. Mishr said the contract would help boost average daily volumes in the spot foreign-exchange market to
$10 billion or more from $4 billion to $5 billion now.
"It will be an additional tool for a lot of customers," said Ashit Parekh, head of foreign-exchange trading at
Indusind Bank in Mumbai.
Indian companies have become more outward-looking as they acquire foreign companies to diversify their
geographic risk. Individuals also are buying assets abroad, aided by average economic growth of 8.8% a year
for the past five years. The economic boom has prompted many investment banks to set up shop in India.
Until now, those who wanted to hedge could use the local over-the-counter forwards market, which requires
investors to hold the underlying assets. The new contract will allow players to take a speculative position on
the rupee.
Offshore players have long been able to use the nondeliverable dollar-rupee forward contracts available in
Singapore. The Dubai Gold &Commodities Exchange recently started a rupee futures contract. Although it
hasn't attracted heavy interest, fear of losing business to offshore financial centers likely spurred the central
bank to allow rupee futures.
Two other Indian exchanges -- the Bombay Stock Exchange and the Multi Commodity Exchange -- are
seeking to list similar contracts. Central-bank strictures on the new currency contracts may mean that
volume remains light until the central bank is comfortable enough to loosen the rules.
"I don't see a surge in volumes initially. I see a gradual increase," Standard Chartered's Mr. Mishr said.
The contracts, which must be settled in rupees, have a `maximum maturity of 12 months, shorter than those
available on other currencies in other financial centers.
The market is open only to local individuals and institutions as well as foreign firms registered in India. That
restriction is intended to keep away big foreign funds that the central bank fears could cause unwanted
volatility.
The central bank also kept the size of the contracts small, at $1,000 each, and imposed position limits of $5
million for clients of banks and brokerages and $25 million for trading members. That will limit the activities of
large banks and corporates that could have provided the kind of liquidity that gives validity to such contracts.
