Small Business Link: Today's Topic: Coping With Inflation / Shifting product lines in the face of rising commodity prices
Small Business Link: Today's Topic: Coping With Inflation / Shifting product lines in the face of rising
commodity prices
By Simona Covel
1,237 words
21 August 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
B6
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Jeweler Diversifies to Bring Back Sales Luster
---
John Christian Creates Cheaper Line and New Brand Amid High Gold Prices and Weak Economy
All that glitters is not gold, William Shakespeare cautioned. These days, it might turn out to be Platanium.
Platanium -- a stainless-steel alloy whose name is meant to invoke the luster of platinum and the strength of
titanium -- is a new metal offered by John Christian, a small custom-jewelry company in Austin, Texas.
John Christian's new product line is a direct response to something affecting all sorts of businesses: soaring
commodity prices. Gold rose to more than $1,000 an ounce early this year before settling back close to the
$800 mark recently -- still double where it was three years ago. Such prices put gold out of reach for many
customers, who also are cutting back in a weak economy.
This double-whammy prompted John Christian to do something innovative: It gave a stainless-steel product a
fancy name, crafted a new brand around it and marketed it as a lower-cost alternative to pricey gold.
"People are still going to get married and love their kids and celebrate special times in their lives, but the
jewelry's going to have to change," says Wes Weaver, John Christian's chief executive. Currently, nearly half
of the firm's orders come from the new, cheaper product line.
Product diversification can help a small company survive -- and grow -- especially when it is dependent on a
product that becomes uncompetitive thanks to a change in the economy, such as rising prices. It allows a
business to balance economic risks and reach new customers. But small firms that create a new product line
also run the risk of muddling their brand's identity -- especially those that have limited brand recognition to
begin with. And high-end companies that extend into lower price ranges or less-fancy materials in particular
risk alienating customers.
A small company with limited brand recognition "doesn't want to compromise the reputation of the premium
product," says Sasha Strauss, managing director at Innovation Protocol, a Los Angeles-based brand
consulting company.
But if a firm is able to "isolate and protect" its higher-status brand while leveraging an existing manufacturing
platform and reaching a new audience, he adds, the results can be gratifying.
Executives at John Christian considered that risk. But the company, which sells personalized jewelry through
its Web site, was in trouble.
As metal costs rose, they raised prices. Custom rings that sold for $599 in 2003 or 2004 gradually crept up to
a current price of around $900. For 2006, the company posted $6 million in revenue. In 2007, as the economy
weakened and retail spending tapered off, revenue slipped to $5 million. Profit margins were lower because
of commodity-price jumps that outpaced retail-price increases.
"I was fighting to survive," Mr. Weaver says.
It's a fight that is reverberating across the jewelry-manufacturing industry, which is made up mostly of small
companies. As metal prices marched higher, many of those companies set themselves apart by offering
custom work, says Gerry Davies, managing editor of the MJSA Journal, a publication put out by the
Manufacturing Jewelers &Suppliers of America, a Providence, R.I., trade group. But as economic concerns

--- Page 157 ---
Page 157 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
mounted, creativity and custom service weren't enough for many of the upstarts, which now are looking to
alternative metals and lighter, cheaper jewelry styles like mesh and filigree.
Mr. Weaver knew John Christian needed to find an advantage in its small size. "So many companies have
gone out of business, but we can turn on a dime," he says. The firm experimented with its product line,
increasing the number of lower-cost sterling-silver items in its lineup. But margins were lower, and the soft
metal was harder to work with.
So he considered other options. Titanium can't be cast in a wax mold, so it wouldn't work for custom
engraved products. Palladium was nearly as expensive as gold and didn't have much name recognition.
Then he thought about stainless steel: It's inexpensive and doesn't scratch much or tarnish.
The company experimented with a stainless-steel ring line targeting military personnel, emblazoned with the
seal of the Army, Navy, Marines or Air Force. Men were immediately receptive to the idea of an
industrial-strength metal. "I've got a big, burly Marine -- no problem," Mr. Weaver says, "The real question
was, 'Can I sell a stainless-steel ring to a man to give to his wife, and can I get a woman to buy it?'"
In a brainstorming session, executives came up with the name Platanium -- positing that the alloy looks like
platinum and is hard like titanium. They felt the name connoted quality. Immediately, Mr. Weaver applied for a
trademark.
John Christian built its 10-year-old business selling custom items in the $400 to $700 range -- reaching out to
high-end customers through ads in the New Yorker and Smithsonian magazines. Afraid those customers
would be put off by a $169 stainless-steel ring, the company decided to keep Platanium separate. It started a
new brand, called Carved Creations, with a different Web address (www.ccforlife.com).
Carved Creations launched in April, ahead of Mother's Day, offering engraved jewelry in Platanium and
sterling silver. All of the items cost less than $200.
For weeks, Mr. Weaver spent most of his time building the new site, guiding the making of the jewelry
prototypes and posting product photos to the site.
The company spent $30,000 upfront on ads that ran in April, May and June -- the biggest expense in starting
the new line. Instead of high-brow magazines, Carved Creations ads appear in USA Today and US Weekly
among others -- publications whose readers tend to be younger and fall into lower income brackets.
Key for the small company: The ordering process for John Christian and Carved Creations items is the same.
The company didn't need to install new software or systems. A computer prints a nearly identical invoice that
says which product line the order belongs to.
So far, the company has sold about $100,000 in Carved Creations jewelry. In the weeks following the new
brand's launch, 30% of customers selected the silver option for their purchase -- a proportion Mr. Weaver
wanted to reduce because of the difficulty in working with silver and its tendency to tarnish.
So the company began offering a lifetime guarantee on the Platanium. Mr. Weaver says it was easy to do so
because of stainless steel's natural durability and resistance to scratching. Sterling items, in contrast, come
with a 30-day warranty. With that change, some customers' resistance fell away. Today, 95% of Carved
Creations orders are for Platanium.
Still, some customers are wary, Mr. Weaver says. The staff fields plenty of calls from potential buyers asking
what exactly Platanium is. "When you get a customer on the phone and you explain what it is," he says, "they
pick it every time."
Old-line jeweler acquaintances have given him a hard time about the cleverly titled alloy, Mr. Weaver admits.
But, he adds, many of those jewelers "are getting killed" trying to sell high-priced gold in a tough economy.

Olympics -- Beijing Olympics 2008: No Joke: What Walks Like It's Full of Duck? --- Answer: Freeloaders Who Quack With Joy
Olympics -- Beijing Olympics 2008: No Joke: What Walks Like It's Full of Duck? --- Answer:
Freeloaders Who Quack With Joy
By Roger Thurow and Juliet Ye
898 words
20 August 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Beijing -- A vision came to Hu Shengqiang when he heard this city would host the Olympics: A duck should
be the mascot.
Why a duck? "Beijing roast duck is the most famous representative of the city," he insists. When you hear
Beijing, he says, you think duck.
Alas, Olympic officials didn't think duck. They settled on five ambiguous creatures as mascots. Mr. Hu,
looking wounded, shrugs and says, "There were many considerations involved, I suppose."
But don't feel too bad for Mr. Hu. For he had a second Olympic dream. "We sensed," he says, "that we could
make a fortune."
Mr. Hu, you see, is deputy general manager of Beijing Golden Star Duck Center, which bills itself as the
largest purveyor of ducks to the Olympic flock. Back in 2001, when the Games were awarded to Beijing,
Golden Star was supplying about 5,000 ducks a day. These days it delivers as many as 13,000 ducks daily to
the city's roast duck emporiums, which are stuffed day and night with Olympic and corporate schmoozers
clamoring for Beijing's signature dish, known here as kao ya and in other places as Peking Duck.
"There is so much demand to satisfy," says Mr. Hu, smiling as broadly as Golden Star's own mascot, a jolly
duck with a red bowtie.
Five hundred of those ducks make their way each day to Da Dong restaurant in the city's business district.
Before the Olympics, waiters carved up about 350 ducks a day. Then in May and June, the calls for
reservations began, particularly from Olympic sponsors hosting large groups. Last week, a request for a
corporate reservation was met with an animated head shake by Zhang Zhaohui, Da Dong's manager.
"All booked up," he says.
Mr. Zhang won't name any of the guests -- "client confidentiality," he says -- but one wasn't shy about posing
with staff and penning a note of gratitude.
"You deserve a gold medal," scribbled Juan Antonio Samaranch, former president of the International
Olympic Committee who oversaw Beijing's selection in 2001. His photo and note, dated the day of these
Games' Opening Ceremonies hang on a wall beyond the wood-fired brick ovens where dozens of ducks
roast.
An additional 600 ducks are dispatched daily to the Athletes' Village, where the main dining hall features a
Beijing Roast Duck station. The original catering plans called for 300 ducks a day. But "as the roast duck is
very popular to athletes, we doubled the number," says Sha Yufeng, director of the Olympic Working
Department of the Beijing Tourism Group, associated with the Quanjude Group and one of the domestic food
suppliers for the Games.
Quanjude opened its first roast duck restaurant in Beijing in 1864. At its eatery off the main shopping street
near the Forbidden City, 1,000 diners a day devour some 300 ducks.
The menu offers a "duck feast" fit for a Qing emperor who was on the throne when the first Quanjude opened:
mustard duck webs, duck tongue and cuttlefish egg soup, flame-touched duck hearts and stir-fried duck
intestines with mushrooms. Most of the Olympic crowd just order the roast duck.
Like the group of 30 Japanese visitors dining at Quanjude as part of an Olympics package. "In Japan, when
people think of Beijing, the only thing they think of is Beijing duck," says Ryosuke Kanie, a tour leader with

--- Page 159 ---
Page 159 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Japan Travel Bureau, JTB, as he settled the bill for their feast with stacks of 100 yuan notes. A roast duck
costs about 200 yuan, or about $30.
If a gold medal were awarded for kao ya, as Mr. Samaranch suggested, one would surely go to Da Dong's
Super Lean Roast Duck. Five years ago, with the invasion of Olympic hordes looming, the proprietor and his
chefs set out to solve a problem that had long bedeviled duck roasters. Since kao ya first appeared in a
cookbook during the Yuan Dynasty (1206-1368), chefs have struggled to keep the meat moist beneath crispy
skin while not crippling consumers with an overdose of cholesterol.
Mr. Hu says it all starts down on the farm. Golden Star raises six million ducks a year at farms on the outskirts
of town. Not all of them make the roast duck grade.
"The perfect duck should be authentic," he says. That means purebred from roast duck parents; big white
feathers with a yellow bill and feet; about 42 days old; and about seven pounds. To build up the right amount
of fat, the ducks are fed four times a day. "Maybe corn, maybe some beans," Mr. Hu says of the feed mix. He
will reveal no more.
But he does speak of a new dream. He hopes to build on the Olympic momentum by promoting "the Beijing
duck culture," to take it beyond the capital city to feed the burgeoning Chinese middle class and maybe even
to foreign markets like America, where he knows a clash with the mighty chicken looms.
This fall, Golden Star plans to open a newer, bigger farm in a neighboring province. Says Mr. Hu: "People
need more ducks."

Dollar Revival Reaps Roses and Thorns --- Stocks Likely to Gain on Mergers, Profits; Caution for Commodities and Exporters
Dollar Revival Reaps Roses and Thorns --- Stocks Likely to Gain on Mergers, Profits; Caution for
Commodities and Exporters
By Jeff D. Opdyke and Craig Karmin
956 words
18 August 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SUDDENLY, IT IS all about the Benjamins. But it won't necessarily get any easier to make a buck.
After struggling for several years, the dollar is back in vogue. It has gained 8% against the euro in the past
month. On Friday, the dollar rose again and one euro equals $1.4673. Last month, a euro was at $1.60, and
bullish analysts say a euro could be $1.40 by next summer. The dollar has rallied the same amount against
the British pound and a bit less against the yen.
Behind the surge: slowing economies across Europe. Data released last week show the euro-zone economy
contracted 0.2% in the second quarter, the first decline since before the euro's introduction in 1999. Falling oil
prices have helped, too, since softer energy costs reduce inflationary pressures and make it easier for the
European Central Bank to begin cutting interest rates to deal with the slipping economy. Earlier this
summer, investors were betting the ECB would raise rates to combat inflation.
"The expectations for Europe have worsened and that's better news for the dollar," says David Wyss, chief
economist at Standard &Poor's. Though longer-term fundamental challenges still face the dollar, expectations
are growing that the greenback will continue a slow climb for at least the next year.
Currency moves have broad and often conflicting impacts on interest rates, corporate profits, commodity
prices, and mergers and acquisitions, particularly if investors believe the trend will continue. Here's how a
rising dollar could play out:
For much of the U.S. stock market, the stronger dollar is a soothing tonic. The buck's rise should help quell
domestic inflation by making imports and commodities cheaper. That could boost profits and reduce worries
that interest rates will rise. It can also make U.S. stocks more attractive to foreign investors, who would get a
boost to their returns when they are converted to their home currencies. But a stronger dollar could hurt
U.S.-based multinational companies such as General Electric Co. and Coca-Cola Co., because profits earned
overseas will be worth less in dollars.
These companies already are facing earnings pressure from the economic weakness that is spreading from
the U.S. to Europe and Japan.
When the dollar was in the dumps, U.S.-based multinationals didn't complain because the goods and
services they sold overseas brought in an increasing number of greenbacks. That flowed through to the
bottom line. That is the short-term impact. If the dollar continues to strengthen, the boom in exports that the
U.S. has experienced in recent years could end as U.S.-made goods get more expensive.
Foreign stocks in the short term will be less attractive to U.S. investors if the dollar keeps rising because
gains in their share price will be offset by losses on their currency.
For commodities investors, a stronger dollar is another reason for caution. Commodities are falling in large
part because of weakening global demand. But because major commodities are denominated in dollars, a
strong dollar means they are more expensive for non-U.S. buyers and that could further damp demand and
push down prices.
Gold, too, is likely to suffer from a stronger dollar, though global inflation and economic woes could mitigate
the decline. Even more than most commodities, gold tends to trade inversely with the dollar -- and has given
up all the gains it had accumulated this year.
Some bullish analysts and investors expect gold to stem its declines because of the continuing world-wide
threat of inflation. The deepening economic turmoil around the world also reinforces gold's safe-haven
appeal.

--- Page 161 ---
Page 161 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
By damping inflation, a strong dollar is a clear benefit for Treasury bonds, which could get another boost if
foreign buyers snap them up to play the rising dollar. The bond-market volatility of the past year will likely be
the driving force behind moves in more credit-sensitive bonds.
Worries that the dollar could continue strengthening have already boosted cross-border M&A activity.
U.S.-based targets are still cheap -- thanks to the nearly seven-year bear market in the dollar -- but are
suddenly getting more expensive to foreign acquirers. That could be "a strong enough factor to hasten a
decision," says Boon Sim, head of mergers and acquisitions at Credit Suisse.
The dollar's turnaround played a role in expediting some recent trans-Atlantic deals, including a bid by
Switzerland's Roche Holding AG to buy the shares in Genentech Inc. it doesn't already own, and Belgian
brewer InBev NV's takeover of Anheuser Busch Cos., according to people familiar with the transactions.
One sector that seems ripe for foreign invasion is the U.S. banking industry. Last week, Japan's Mitsubishi
UFJ Financial Group Inc. made an unsolicited $3 billion offer to acquire the 35% of UnionBanCal Corp. that it
doesn't already own.
The dollar has been mixed in the past month against currencies from some fast-growing emerging
economies, and some investors expect those currencies to stay strong. The Chinese currency, which has
risen a bit against the dollar, has been kept artificially low by the government and is expected to continue
strengthening against the greenback.
Michael Hasenstab, co-director of Franklin Templeton Investments' international bond department, says
Asian currencies are "still likely to outperform against the dollar." The firm's global bond fund exited
euro-denominated positions at the end of 2007 but has building bullish exposures to the Singapore and
Taiwanese dollars and the Malaysian ringgit.

We'll All Pay for the Fed's Loose Money Follies
We'll All Pay for the Fed's Loose Money Follies
By Benn Steil
693 words
18 August 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
In the dozen or so years until 2007, it had become as close to a global orthodoxy in economic policy making
as we ever see: Central banks should target a low and stable rate of inflation.
This replaced earlier orthodoxies -- such as that central banks should maintain a fixed exchange rate with an
ounce of gold, which was abandoned in 1971. Though inflation targeting left far more latitude for
government officials to expand the money supply, it too ultimately proved too great a shackle on the exercise
of central bank wisdom.
The U.S. Federal Reserve, the European Central Bank, the Bank of England and other rich-country central
banks have generally made 2% inflation, give or take a smidge, the touchstone of good performance. Fed
officials have for 20 years paid public obeisance to their statutory "dual mandate," to maximize employment
as well as stabilize prices. But in practice, until recently, they treated it much like a mildly embarrassing
biblical injunction that could be safely ignored, if not repudiated.
Yet one of the great attractions of inflation targeting was that it only appeared to constrain central bankers'
discretion. Other objectives which today's central bankers actually think are far more important -- in particular,
keeping growth of that great aggregate of aggregates, "gross domestic product," above 0% -- can be safely
pursued in place of price stability.
This is because the inflation target is what's called a "medium-term objective." The Fed is actually free to
slash its key interest rate from 5.25% to 2%, stuffing the world with dollars, even as inflation soars past 5.6%
(a 17-year high), because the public's inflation expectations will supposedly remain "well anchored." That is,
we will eventually stop charging and paying each other ever higher prices for stuff, in spite of the flood of Fed
money, because we know that once the Fed prints enough of it to fix our problems it will get really mad over
the inflation we produced and target it with a vengeance.
Unless, of course, it hurts the GDP number, in which case the "medium term" will just be a bit longer. You get
the picture.
The irony of the collapse of inflation targeting's intellectual edifice is that it has long been championed by
Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke, not to mention departing Fed Governor Frederick Mishkin, who recently made
a call for "establishing a transparent and credible commitment to a specific numerical [inflation] objective."
This after siding with the doves on every rate vote, even as inflation soared to triple his preferred target.
The logic behind central bank discretion is that the economy is like a giant factory churning out 100 widgets
a year, all of which are happily bought by consumers without prices rising. A sudden "exogenous shock" cuts
demand to 98 widgets. But the central bank can then print money to induce consumers to buy up the two
excess widgets, thereby stopping the factory from cutting production capacity and causing a "recession." It
claws back the excess money when "equilibrium" is restored.
But what if this analogy is deeply flawed? What if the economy is much more like two factories than one? One
factory produces, say, real-estate widgets, and the other produces everything else. If consumers decide they
want fewer real-estate widgets and more of some other widgets, it will take time and resources for capacity to
shift from the first factory to the second. "GDP" growth will decline during that time. But the process is normal
and desirable. By printing more money, the central bank only makes it longer and more painful, not least by
producing significant and prolonged inflation.
This is the serious mistake the Fed has made. And in so doing, it has probably killed the last great hope for a
sound, durable global fiat money system -- inflation targeting.
---

--- Page 163 ---
Page 163 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Steil is director of international economics at the Council on Foreign Relations, and a co-author of
"Money, Markets and Sovereignty" (Yale University Press, forthcoming).
(See related letter: "Action, Not Talk" -- WSJ September 4, 2008)

Gold Futures Tumble Down 'Metals' Stand
Commodities Report
Gold Futures Tumble Down 'Metals' Stand
By Carolyn Cui
586 words
16 August 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold will have to settle for the bronze.
Through Friday, gold futures have given up all the gains they had accumulated this year, now 5.9% off
where they began.
Gold's sister metal, silver, has fallen harder, and plunged 9.9% Friday. Though both crude-oil futures and the
broad Dow Jones-AIG Commodity Index fell Friday, they are up on the year, 19% and 1.2%, respectively.
"There's been a lot of surprise by the depth of the reversal" in gold, said Thomas Winmill, who manages
Midas Fund, a precious-metals fund.
Judging by some indicators, gold should be holding up. Inflation world-wide remains a threat, and the
continuing credit crunch and spreading economic worries should make gold an attractive safe-haven bet.
But the recent rise in the dollar caught investors off guard. Meanwhile, commodities as a group have been
selling off, and jewelry producers have recoiled -- second-quarter jewelry demand for gold fell 24% this year
from the same period a year ago.
Gold futures Friday fell 2.7%, or $22.20, to settle at $786 a troy ounce on the Comex division of the New
York Mercantile Exchange. It finished its fifth losing week in a row.
Even gold watchers have been caught off guard. Although total demand fell 19% in the second quarter from
a year ago in terms of tonnage, according to the World Gold Council, demand rose 9% in terms of value, as
gold prices were up then versus the year-ago period. Yet "the market has behaved as if it's a totally different
world," said George Milling-Stanley of the World Gold Council.
Gold miners have spent billions of dollars to remove price hedges out of fear gold prices would go higher.
This year's second quarter marked the 25th consecutive quarterly reduction of miners' hedging programs,
according to VM Group, a London commodities-research firm.
Now, these companies are being punished for being bullish, as their profit margins will be squeezed by falling
gold prices. AngloGold Ashanti Ltd., the world's third-largest gold producer in terms of production, spent
$977 million reducing its hedge book by 3.15 million ounces during the last quarter, according to the South
African company's financial statements.
Year to date, AngloGold's shares are down 35%, and other companies unwinding hedges such as Barrick
Gold Corp., the No. 1 producer, and No. 2 Newmont Mining Corp. are down 24% and 15%, respectively.
Meanwhile, some analysts are "revising short and medium term forecasts as fast as they can keep track of
the proverbial falling knife," wrote Jon Nadler, a senior analyst at Kitco Bullion Dealers Montreal.
On Aug. 7, Goldman Sachs Group Inc. said it was maintaining its long-term price forecast for gold of $810 an
ounce. It cited stagnant mine-production growth and strong demand from consumers and investors. But
Thursday, Goldman slashed its forecasts, lowering its short-term target on gold by 16% to $745 an ounce,
and its 12-month forecast by 8.6% to $740, citing strength in the dollar.
Earlier this month, Standard Chartered Bank of the U.K. pushed back its forecast for $1,000 gold from the
first half of 2009 to the second half of that year.
Bulls remain. Citigroup Inc. analyst John Hill wrote Thursday that gold will head into a seasonally strong
period. "Longer term, we would not be surprised to see gold double from current levels."

Dollar's Rise Could Damp Inflation
Dollar's Rise Could Damp Inflation
By Jon Hilsenrath, Gregory Zuckerman and Craig Karmin
1,283 words
16 August 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
A stronger U.S. dollar reduces pressure on the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates. A graphic that
accompanied a page-one article Saturday about the dollar's rebound against other currencies incorrectly said
the move pressures the Fed to raise interest rates.
(WSJ Aug. 18, 2008)
(END)
The U.S. dollar marched higher again on Friday, continuing a development that could ease inflationary
pressures but also could slow a U.S. export boom.
Just a couple of months ago, policy makers were alarmed about how far the dollar had fallen. Now evidence
is building that its seven-year slide may be ending.
On Friday, the euro fell to 1.4673 against the dollar, and the dollar fetched 110.51 yen. The British pound has
fallen against the dollar for 11 straight days, its worst such streak since at least 1982. Over the past month,
the U.S. currency has gained about 8% against the euro and the pound, and nearly 6% against the yen.
When a currency strengthens, it's usually a sign of health in the underlying economy. In this case, the dollar's
rally is a sign of weakness in other economies. Reports in recent days showed that the economies of Japan
and Europe contracted in the second quarter, and the U.K. slowed. It's now looking less likely that the rest of
the world will be insulated from U.S. economic ills.
The dollar's latest rise is closely tied to recent declines in oil and other commodity prices. As economies in the
rest of the world slow, demand for raw materials appears to be waning, which is taking pressure off
commodity prices. These goods are typically priced in dollars. As the U.S. currency strengthens, commodity
producers have less incentive to increase their prices, further easing the upward pressure on prices.
Oil fell $1.24 a barrel Friday, closing at $113.77 in New York trading. Over the past six weeks, the price of
crude has fallen nearly 22%. Prices for other commodities, including copper and corn, have also fallen. The
price of gold, which is sometimes used as an inflation hedge, is down about 18% over the past five weeks.
"The markets now recognize that commodity-linked price inflation due to a weak dollar and a strong global
economy is yesterday's worry," says Robert Barbera, chief economist of Investment Technology Group Inc.
The development has wide-ranging implications for financial markets and the global economy. It is generally
good news for central bankers worried about the upward drift of inflation, particularly in the U.S.
In June, Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke issued a rare public declaration on currencies, saying the
dollar's steep fall was feeding inflation pressures by pushing up the cost of imported goods. On Thursday,
the Labor Department reported that U.S. consumer prices were up 5.6% in July from a year ago, the biggest
increase in 17 years.
A stronger currency would help to relieve that pressure, by taking some steam out of the rise of commodity
prices and other imported goods. That, in turn, "reduces the probability of an interest-rate increase in the
U.S.," says Jens Nordvig, currency strategist for Goldman Sachs.
But there will be plenty of negative fallout from the currency's unexpected turn. Hedge funds that trade
currencies, oil, gold and other commodities could feel the effects right away. Lots of funds became energy
bulls earlier this summer, and when oil prices dropped, they took big losses. Hedge funds that wagered
against the dollar have also taken losses.

--- Page 167 ---
Page 167 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The currency market is a $3.2 trillion dollar a day market, with everyone from Wall Street titans to corporate
treasurers making bets. Many traders had been convinced that the dollar's long decline would continue and
that oil would keep rising. Now many hedge funds and other investors are scrambling to reduce their
commodities positions. Gold has given up all its gains from this year, and silver has fallen even harder. In
addition to being hit by the stronger dollar, the metals have been hurt by falling jewelry demand.
July was the worst month in eight years for the hedge-fund business, according to Hedge Fund Research, a
Chicago firm that tracks the business. Funds lost 2.8% on average, worse than the 1% drop in the Standard
&Poor's 500, according to the firm. So-called macro hedge funds, which bet on broad trends in the dollar,
commodities and other areas, were the biggest losers, dropping 5.5%. The losses appear to be continuing:
The funds are down another 3.7% so far in August, bringing the six-week loss to more than 9%.
"Hedge funds have maintained exposure to commodities and global currencies" despite the recent falloff in
commodity prices, says Ken Heinz, president of HFR. "Those were a big part of the gains for these funds for
the past year, but now it's costing them."
Among the hedge funds losing money lately: New York-based Jana Partners LLC's flagship $5 billion fund,
which entered July up more than 4% for the year, fell 9% during the month, hurt in part by falling prices of
energy stocks. The Ospraie Fund has declined by more than 20% this year due to commodity bets, dropping
13% in July alone.
A stronger dollar, if sustained over a longer term, could put the U.S. economy on shakier ground by raising
the cost of exports. Without the improving U.S. trade position, the U.S. economy would have contracted in the
second quarter. Exports grew at a robust 9% annual rate during the quarter, helped along by the cumulative
effects of the dollar's weakening in recent years.
Several economists said Friday it was too soon to declare the U.S. export boom over. Despite the dollar's
recent gains, it has a long way to go to return to levels seen at the start of the decade. The greenback is still
44% lower versus the euro than it was in October 2000, when the euro was worth only 83 cents. The dollar is
also 23% weaker than it was against the pound back then, and is 16% below where it traded against the yen
at the end of 2001.
Moreover, some economists see more room for growth in exports to developing countries. In a report Friday,
AllianceBernstein economist Joseph Carson estimated that emerging markets are worth $10 trillion and are
growing at a 5% annual rate, which could help keep the export boom going, if at a slower pace.
In Europe, the strong euro had become a burden to some exporters. EADS, parent company of plane maker
Airbus, expanded a cost-cutting program because of the high euro, including outsourcing more production to
low-cost countries. Airbus incurs most of its costs in euros, but prices its aircraft in dollars. A sustained drop
in the euro could help Airbus and other exporters by making their products cheaper for overseas buyers.
The European Central Bank has been preoccupied with inflation. While a stronger dollar could take some
pressure off commodity prices, it could raise the cost of other goods imported to Europe.
"The euro had gotten to the point that on a fundamental basis it was overvalued by 30%," says Michael
Hasenstab, co-director for the international bond department at Franklin-Templeton. When you match that
with the suddenly sour economic news coming from Europe, he says, "you tend to get very quick moves in
the currency. That was the catalyst."

The shift of money out of commodities and into some beaten-down stock sectors over the past month has
fueled a rally in exchange-traded funds that were among the biggest losers in the first half of the year.
In particular, short-oil funds as well as ETFs tracking financial and home-builder sectors have been among
the top performers during the recent correction in commodity prices.
Among exchange-traded funds and notes, the top five performers for the month ended Aug. 13 were products
designed to give leveraged, short exposure to commodities prices, according to data from Morningstar Inc.
PowerShares DB Crude Oil Double Short ETN, PowerShares DB Commodity Double Short ETN,
PowerShares DB Agriculture Double Short ETN, PowerShares DB Gold Double Short ETN and
PowerShares DB Base Metals Double Short ETN have gained at least 30% over the past month.
PowerShares DB Crude Oil Double Short -- an exchange-traded note geared to provide twice the inverse, or
opposite, of crude prices -- has risen nearly 50% as oil prices have cooled, at least for now.
Meanwhile, stock-sector ETFs that were pummeled during the first half of 2008 on the problems in the
housing and credit markets have benefited from the rotation out of commodities.
KBW Regional Banking and SPDR S&P Homebuilders ETF are up 26.8% and 25.4%, respectively, over the
past month, according to Morningstar.
The moves suggest investors are at least brightening on the prospects for these two unloved sectors.
"The weakest sectors of the first half of 2008 have turned into the leaders of the most recent rally," wrote Matt
McCall, president of Penn Financial Group, in a recent edition of his client newsletter.
"The most obvious rotation of money has occurred in the commodities arena," he added. "Big money has
been pulled from the sector as the U.S. dollar rallies and the global demand story weighs on the future
outlook."
Precious-metals ETFs have also been hit recently by a higher U.S. dollar. SPDR Gold Shares, a massive
fund that tracks gold prices, has shed 14.3% the past month.
Even before the recent correction in oil prices, there was evidence that investors' enthusiasm for surging
commodity ETFs and ETNs was waning.
In the second quarter, inflows into all U.S.-listed ETFs approached $12 billion, according to Morgan Stanley.
"The largest net cash inflows occurred into ETFs tracking fixed-income and leveraged/inverse ETFs," wrote
Morgan Stanley analysts led by Paul Mazzilli in an Aug. 12 research note. "Inflows slowed for ETFs tracking
commodities, while those tracking large-cap U.S. equities saw the largest outflows."
They observed that ETF flows in the second quarter "seem to have preceded the reversal of the
short-financials/long-energy trade."
Morgan Stanley pointed to strong buying in Select Sector SPDR-Financial, a highly traded ETF that carves
the financial stocks from the S&P 500-stock Index.
Also, investors piled into a leveraged ETF tracking the sector that represents a bullish bet: ProShares Trust
Ultra Financial, which has jumped 16% over the past month.

--- Page 170 ---
Page 170 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Evidence of the trade to short energy and go long financials also appeared in the ProShares Trust UltraShort
Oil &Gas, which had the second highest inflows in the second quarter at $1.8 billion, while the ProShares
Trust UltraShort Financials had net outflows of $1.3 billion," the Morgan Stanley analysts wrote.
Proshares UltraShort Financials, a leveraged bearish fund, has been a hot performer during the credit crunch
and a popular way to bet against financial stocks or hedge.
Since indexed ETFs and ETNs allow investors to easily jump in and out of sectors, more analysts are using
them to track both markets and money flows.
There are now 723 U.S.-listed ETFs with combined average daily trading volume of nearly $90 billion. Even
though money continues to flow into ETFs, total assets have fallen in 2008 due to market declines and stood
at $582 billion as of Aug. 5, according to Morgan Stanley. The data don't include ETNs, which are growing
rapidly.
Yet even though new ETFs continue to hit the market, not every one is a success.
Although 61 new ETFs were launched in the second quarter, there were 12 liquidations, bringing total
liquidations this year to 23, Morgan Stanley said.

The Money Game --- Charging Ahead: With so many credit-card companies targeting small businesses, it pays to find the right one for you
Small Business (A Special Report)
The Money Game --- Charging Ahead: With so many credit-card companies targeting small
businesses, it pays to find the right one for you
By Shara Tibken
1,533 words
11 August 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
R6
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Small business is becoming big business for credit-card issuers -- and that's benefiting people like Jessica
Lim, founder and chief executive of New York design company Sprout Creative Group Inc.
When Ms. Lim was looking to expand her firm, she knew she would need a little extra funding to help cover
monthly expenses.
After doing some research, she opened an American Express Co. Gold Card account in 2003 to purchase
computers, software and office furniture for the company, as well as accrue travel rewards. Ms. Lim was so
happy with her Gold Card that she recently opened an American Express Plum Card account, which gives
her the option to defer payment on the bulk of her monthly bill for a longer period, if needed. Sprout Creative
currently charges about $10,000 of its monthly expenses of $30,000 to $40,000.
"I didn't have to max out personal or business credit cards and put the company into debt so early on, and
that's something that has led to a financially healthy company -- cash in the bank, capital improvements made
when we need them and travel rewards to boot," says Ms. Lim, who estimates her company will generate
$750,000 in revenue this year.
With the consumer market saturated, card issuers and banks are expanding their credit-card offerings for
small businesses in a bid to get in on what they see as an untapped market. Estimates vary, but American
Express says only about 14% of the more than $2 trillion small businesses spend annually is done with
plastic. In addition, businesses generally charge much more than consumers do.
"As more and more issuers and banks realize they're tapped out on consumers, small businesses have
become more of a target," says Curtis Arnold, founder and chief executive of CardRatings.com, a credit-card
information Web site owned by U.S. Citizens for Fair Credit Card Terms Inc., an independent
consumer-advocacy organization. "We've seen a definite proliferation in the past couple years, and it has
really escalated," Mr. Arnold adds.
That means more choices for small-business owners who are looking for additional capital. While the
conventional mind-set is that credit-card debt is bad debt, it is often much easier and faster for start-ups to
open a credit-card account than to secure a line of credit from a bank, says Mr. Arnold. In fact, sometimes it is
the only money a small business can get. It also is a good way to establish credit in a business's name, he
says.
The key to using a credit-card responsibly, financial advisers say, is to not spend money that the company
doesn't have or reasonably expect to have soon. The ramifications of not paying a bill on time may include
steep charges and a higher interest rate. A default also could put the owner's personal credit score in
jeopardy, because he or she may have had to rely on personal credit to open the card account.
With the multitude of cards available -- offering benefits ranging from low interest rates to cash and travel
rewards to longer grace periods -- it can be overwhelming trying to find the one that best suits your
company's needs. But industry experts says the choice among cards gets easier if you take the following
factors into consideration:
SPENDING HABITS
First and foremost, think about how you plan to use the card. What percentage of monthly business expenses
do you expect to charge, and will your company be able to pay the bill in full each month?

--- Page 179 ---
Page 179 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"The basic question is whether the card is needed for paying off [charges] over time or paying them off every
month," says Ben Woolsey, director of marketing and consumer research at CreditCards.com Inc., an online
credit-card marketplace that brings consumers and card issuers together.
For businesses planning to carry over a balance from month to month, a card's annual percentage rate is the
most important factor to consider. For those that aim to pay off charges every month, cards with longer grace
periods or more generous rewards might be more appropriate.
With any card, be aware of what happens if you can't pay the bill in full each month or make a late payment.
Some issuers will take away any rewards earned, in addition to assessing penalties and fees.
Many cards have strict preset spending limits, but others, like those from American Express, have no limits.
With most of the American Express cards, however, the bill has to be paid in full each month.
An exception to that is American Express's Plum Card, which doesn't offer reward points but does allow
holders to defer monthly payments for up to 60 days with no interest if they pay at least 10% of the balance
by the "please pay by" date on their billing statement. Alternatively, card holders with balances of more than
$5,000 can get a 2% discount on their monthly charges by paying the bill in full within the first 10 days of the
end of the billing period. There is, however, an annual fee associated with this card, but it is often waived for
the first year.
For business owners who have good credit and expect to carry a balance from month to month, there are
cards available with low interest rates, and many have teaser rates of 0% for a year, Mr. Arnold says.
QuickBooks Platinum Plus for Business MasterCard, for example, offers a variable annual percentage rate as
low as 6.24%, while Wachovia Corp.'s Wachovia Visa Business without Rewards offers a variable rate as low
as 6.99%.
Advanta Corp.'s Advanta Platinum BusinessCard with Rewards offers a fixed interest rate of 7.99%, with 0%
APR for balance transfers for 15 months. Advanta also has a customizable version of the card on which small
businesses can put their logo or other details. J.P. Morgan Chase &Co.'s Chase Business Rebate Card also
offers a fixed APR of 7.99%.
In a rising interest-rate environment, fixed-rate cards have some advantages, Mr. Arnold says.
REWARDS AND PERKS
After determining how your business is going to use a credit card, focus on finding a card with rewards that
match the needs of your company, insiders say.
"Make sure you're getting as much as possible," says Raymond Joabar, a senior vice president and general
manager at American Express Open, the small-business-products unit of American Express.
If you run a company that involves a lot of travel, for example, look for a card that offers high airline-mile
rewards. Capital One Financial Corp.'s Capital One Visa Business Platinum Card with Preferred No Hassle
Miles offers double miles on travel and entertainment purchases, including airfare, cabs and movie rentals.
For all other purchases, you earn one mile.
If travel isn't a major factor in your business, look for cards that offer cash back. Many cards give 1% to 5%
cash back, but be aware of the fine print. Sometimes rewards are capped, and many cards feature tiered
plans -- they may give a higher percentage of cash back for business-supply purchases or for gas, but a
much lower percentage back for everything else.
"You often have to spend a significant amount to get the full flat percentage back," says Mr. Woolsey of
CreditCards.com. Consider what type of purchases you make the most, and factor that into your decision.
Look for other perks, as well, experts say. Some cards provide benefits just for using the card, such as
discounts on shipping through FedEx Corp., access to airport lounges and hotel upgrades.
Discover Financial Services offers a Discover fee-free PurchaseChecks program where check-writing is
treated like any other purchase. That is a different from many other cards, where check-writing is treated as a
cash advance and subject to hefty fees.
Don't automatically write off cards with annual fees, says Timothy Wyman, a partner at the Center for
Financial Planning Inc., a Southfield, Mich., financial-planning firm. Sometimes they makes sense if what you
get back from the card -- an extra-long grace period, for example -- is worth more to you than the amount you
pay in fees, he says.

--- Page 180 ---
Page 180 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Finally, evaluate what type of credit you have and whether you have established credit in your business's
name. Credit cards that report to business bureaus such as Dun &Bradstreet instead of personal-credit
bureaus "allow businesses to build credit in that business's name," Mr. Arnold says. That is "very important in
terms of capital and financing needs going forward," he says.
While this type of information may be difficult to obtain, it is worth asking about and helpful if your personal
credit is "less than stellar," Mr. Arnold says.

shara.tibken@dowjones.com.

Could OZ Find Its Heart? --- Improved Picture For Copper, Zinc Might Boost Miner

Could OZ Find Its Heart? --- Improved Picture For Copper, Zinc Might Boost Miner
By Stephen Bell

The Wall Street Journal

PERTH, Australia -- OZ Minerals, created from the merger of copper-gold miner Oxiana and zinc miner
Zinifex, has suffered a shaky first five weeks in its new form. Since the deal took effect July 1, OZ has lost
nearly 30% of its value.
A comparison with early March, when the 50-50 merger was unveiled, produces an even bleaker picture. The
combined market value of Oxiana and Zinifex back then was 12 billion Australian dollars, or about US$11
billion. Now OZ Minerals is valued at less than half of that -- about A$5.4 billion -- as investors fret about
tumbling base-metal prices and cost pressures.
The slump has been so pronounced that OZ Chief Executive Andrew Michelmore said recently he will
consider buying back the stock as an alternative to making acquisitions.
OZ shares closed Wednesday at A$1.74, up 6.7%, amid a broad recovery in the Australian stock market after
a Tuesday rout -- in which OZ stock tumbled 13% -- caused by falling commodity prices. OZ shares closed at
A$2.47 on July 1.
Many Australian analysts say they believe a rebound is in store in the next 12 months for the country's
fourth-largest miner by market capitalization. Of seven major stock brokers surveyed by Dow Jones
Newswires, six have "buy" or "overweight" recommendations, with one "underweight" call. Most say OZ will
benefit from a combination of increased copper production and steadier zinc prices.
"We think that zinc prices are close to bottoming," said UBS analyst Jo Battershill, who upgraded his rating
from "neutral" to "buy" July 23, with a 12-month estimate of A$2.90 a share -- 67% above Wednesday's close.
He sees zinc averaging 95 U.S. cents a pound next year from 79 U.S. cents now.
He noted that OZ will increase copper output significantly later this year when its A$1.1 billion Prominent Hill
mine in South Australia state enters production. That will provide a boost as copper prices, down 10% since
early March, have held up much better than zinc's. Once the mine kicks in, copper will account for about half
of OZ's revenue, the company forecasts. Zinc will supply most of the rest, with gold, nickel, lead and silver
contributing lesser amounts.
Latest Oil-Price Drop
Boosts Asian Markets
Another drop in oil prices helped push up Asian stock markets, while Australian shares got a lift from
indications that the next move from the country's central bank might be to lower interest rates.
Hong Kong was closed because of a typhoon warning.

The pan-European Dow Jones Stoxx 600 index rose 0.9% to 287.66, its highest close since June 30.
Australia's S&P/ASX 200 soared 3.1% to 4969.10 in the first full trading session after the central bank left
interest rates unchanged and indicated rate cuts might be coming as demand slowed. Commonwealth Bank
of Australia jumped 6.3% and Westpac Banking rose 5.3%.
In TOKYO, the Nikkei Stock Average of 225 companies gained 2.6% to 13254.89. Sony rose 5.7% and
Canon added 5%.
In SHANGHAI, the Shanghai Composite gained 1.1% to 2719.37.

Muslim Land Joins Ranks of Tigers --- Turkey's Islamist-Leaning Leaders Embrace Free Markets
Muslim Land Joins Ranks of Tigers --- Turkey's Islamist-Leaning Leaders Embrace Free Markets
By Andrew Higgins and Farnaz Fassihi
2,386 words
6 August 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ANTALYA, Turkey -- When an Islamist-leaning political party took charge of Turkey six years ago, this vibrant
Mediterranean resort town feared a bumpy ride for a local economy driven in part by booze and bikinis.
Today, says Ahmet Barut, a hotel magnate here, the only real question is whether the town can sustain an
unprecedented economic boom. He's not keen on the teetotaling habits of the governing party's leaders, nor
the headscarves worn by their wives, but he applauds a key part of their record: "They are good at
economics."
From tourism and tomato growing to car making, Turkey has prospered far more under an Islam-tinged
government than it did under some previous, ardently secular administrations more in tune with the often
decidedly un-Islamic ways of many Turkish businesspeople.
By nearly every measure, Turkey's economy is now much stronger than it was when the Justice and
Development Party, or AK Party, won elections in 2002. Annual growth since then has averaged around
6.5%, up from an annual average of roughly 2.5% over the previous six-year period.
Turkey still faces various headwinds. Economic momentum has been slowed by the global credit crunch, and
the country is running a sizable current-account deficit. A spasm of political uncertainty this year over
headscarves didn't help matters. The AK Party set off the turbulence with an effort to let female college
students wear headscarves on campus -- a potent symbol of conservative Islam, and a touchstone issue in
Turkey, which is constitutionally secular. Opponents used the headscarf flap to try (unsuccessfully) to outlaw
the AK Party, saying it harbors a hidden agenda to turn Turkey into an Islamic state resembling Iran.
Despite hiccups like these, the economy shows scant sign of slipping back into its earlier doldrums. "Numbers
don't lie," says Antalya's mayor, Menderes Turel, an AK Party member. He boasts of new investment, new
sewage pipes and a new airport terminal, but also hails a phenomenon not generally esteemed in Muslim
lands: Alcohol sales -- turbocharged by tourists, restaurants and a raucous club scene -- are up.
Nearly all Turks are Muslim. But Islam and its symbols have been banished from spheres of state here since
1923, when Mustafa Kemal Ataturk founded the modern Turkish republic on the ruins of the Ottoman Empire.
Ataturk regarded religion as an obstacle to his vision for Turkey as a modern, Western power.
The AK Party's economic record highlights an intriguing evolution in relations between political Islam and
capitalism. Islamists in the Arab world tend to look askance at the secular ways of their Turkish brethren, but
Turkey's experiment with modern-minded Islam is closely watched in big parts of the Muslim world.
Islam itself has nothing against business. The Islamic prophet Muhammad started out as a merchant, and his
first wife was a successful businesswoman. Asked to fix prices in the bazaar by followers who wanted to buy
goods more cheaply, Muhammad is said to have refused: "Only Allah governs the market."
Over the past century, however, Islam has often served as a vehicle for anticapitalist revolt. Egypt's Sayyid
Qutb, an influential Islamist thinker, crystallized this trend with a 1951 book, "The Battle Between Islam and
Capitalism." Across much of the Muslim world, Islamists denounced the market, not out of deeply held
economic convictions, but mostly in response to corrupt crony capitalism in countries such as Egypt, Algeria
and Indonesia before the fall of Suharto in 1998.
Turkey's AK Party, however, has gone in the opposite direction. It insists it has no desire to challenge
Turkey's secular order. Instead, it sees the solution to many of Turkey's ills in the free-market -- both to open
up and vivify an economy long dominated by a state-coddled elite and also to modernize a political system
forged in the 1930s and still tinged with authoritarianism.
"We favor more competition, productivity and innovation," says Mehmet Simsek, Turkey's minister of state for
economic affairs. "Those with 1930s mind-sets -- statist and inward-looking -- cannot understand this."

--- Page 184 ---
Page 184 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
An observant Muslim married to an American from Wisconsin, Mr. Simsek embodies the AK Party's efforts to
bridge different but, it believes, compatible worlds. Mr. Simsek used to work as an economist for Merrill Lynch
in London and before that at the U.S. Embassy in Ankara. Faith, says the minister, is a personal affair, not a
matter of state.
The success of the AK Party's economic policy, he says, is easily measured: "People are making money."
Private-car sales, he notes, have jumped during his party's tenure from 90,000 a year to around 400,000.
Per-capita gross domestic product has soared from $3,300 in 2002 to more than $10,000.
Turkey's rebound from financial disaster in 2001 -- when the currency went into free fall and the economy
contracted by 6% -- "has surpassed expectations," the International Monetary Fund said in its most recent
detailed review of Turkey's economy, prepared last year before the headscarf ruckus. It credited "sound
macroeconomic policies, a conducive global environment and political stability."
Since the AK Party took over at the end of 2002, exports have more than tripled, foreign investment has
jumped from under $1 billion a year to more than $20 billion, and the number of tourists has more than
doubled. Inflation, out of control for three decades, remains a problem but has fallen sharply to around 10%.
Growth in GDP this year is expected to be around 4.5%, way down from over 9% in 2004, but still robust.
Guided by the IMF, the AK Party has imposed fiscal discipline that eluded its predecessors, accelerated
privatization and trimmed bureaucracy. Recently abolished, for example, was a requirement that any
company with 50 or more employees must hire an ex-convict and a terrorism victim, among various others.
The AK Party has also reactivated a long-stalled drive to join the European Union, pushing through regulatory
and other changes designed to bring Turkey more into line with European norms.
The AK Party's mix of free-market zeal and emphasis on conservative, faith-rooted personal values has
scrambled conventional alignments. In many countries, politicians who back free-market economics are
supported most enthusiastically by the wealthy. Poorer folk tend to be more skeptical.
In Turkey, it has been the other way round. The AK Party's biggest traditional base of support is among
poorer and generally more devout citizens. Many wealthy businesspeople, on the other hand, started out
viewing the party with deep suspicion -- and still do on issues such as headscarves.
Foreign investors were also dubious. The AK Party's Islamic background raised some eyebrows, but one key
worry was that the victors might turn their back on an agreement with the IMF negotiated by the previous
government. Backtracking would likely stoke inflation and derail economic recovery.
Instead of rejecting the IMF package, the new government embraced it and carried out a long list of
IMF-endorsed reforms. "In the market, there was a lot of skepticism," Mr. Simsek said recently in his office in
Ankara, the capital. Over his desk hangs a portrait of Ataturk, the father of modern Turkey.
When the IMF program reached its scheduled end earlier this summer, Lorenzo Giorgianni, the IMF's Turkey
mission chief, said it had contributed to "the most rapid period of economic growth in the recent history of the
Republic."
Meantime, an influential organization of Turkish industrialists, known as TUSIAD, has also shed much of its
early skepticism, at least about Turkey's economic direction. Haluk Tukel, its current secretary general, hails
AK Party economic policy over the past six years as "perfect." But, he adds, many TUSIAD members still
worry that the AK Party has a hidden Islamic agenda.
Such worries grew sharply this year when the government moved to lift a ban on pious female students
covering their hair in class. AK Party has also talked about making adultery a criminal offense.
The opposition, flummoxed by AK Party's economic and electoral successes, has seized on issues like these
to try to win back support. Onur Oymen, vice president of the staunchly secular CHP party, dismisses Mr.
Simsek and other Westernized officials as window dressing. AK Party's ultimate goal, he believes, is an
Islamic theocracy.
"You cannot have a short version of the Quran," he says. "They present themselves as representing modern
life. It is just the opposite."
Nonsense, says Cemil Ipekci, an openly gay Turkish fashion designer who wears gold earrings and
assortments of flamboyant necklaces and bracelets. "They are not radical Islamists. They are conservative,
yes, but not fanatics. Look at me, they socialize with me." He says he and his boyfriend attend state dinners
and parties organized by AK Party ministers. The government-owned airline, Turkish Airlines, hired him to
re-design its decor.

--- Page 185 ---
Page 185 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Levent Kizil, a more mainstream business leader, is also a fan. "They turned our economy round," says the
Istanbul-based owner of a big soft-drinks company. He doesn't want Islam interfering with his lifestyle but
sees scant sign yet of this happening. "I enjoy my secular lifestyle. I like to drink alcohol and gamble and
none of the women in my family wear headscarves," he says, chomping on a Cuban cigar at an Italian
seafood restaurant.
When AK Party took office, Mr. Kizil's company exported just $3 million of soft drinks. The figure is now $12
million. Confident that his domestic and foreign markets will keep growing, Mr. Kizil has invested 25 million
euros, or about $40 million, to renovate two plants.
Turkey's economy still has big problems. One of the biggest is mushrooming current-account deficit, which
stood at around $43 billion for the 12 months leading to May. The current account is the broadest measure of
a nation's trade balance -- and deficits raise worrisome questions about how the country will finance the gap.
Mr. Simsek blames the deficit largely on energy costs. Others point to Turkey's imports of machinery and
other items needed to modernize the economy, and point out that Turkey's current account has been positive
only in times of economic malaise.
Nevertheless, the deficit has spooked the market. There are concerns that a global credit squeeze could hurt
Turkey's ability to borrow the funds it needs. Standard &Poor's Corp., the credit-rating agency, in April revised
its outlook for Turkey from "stable" to "negative." It last week changed this back to "stable" after Turkey's
highest court struck down a request from the chief prosecutor that the ruling AK Party be disbanded for
"antisecular" activities related largely to the headscarf spat.
Turkey has a long history of abrupt swings in economic fortune. An earlier attempt to open up to the world
economy -- and mimic the success of Asia's so-called "tiger economies" -- got off to a promising start in the
1980s. But that export-driven effort petered out and was followed by an era of chronic inflation and
budgetary indiscipline. A big constraint on economic performance has been political instability, caused in part
by a Kurdish minority seeking greater autonomy, as well as the continuing debate over the very nature of the
Turkish state.
Many investors are now waiting to see whether Turkey can reach a new agreement with the IMF. It doesn't
need money from the fund, says Mr. Simsek, and has paid back more than half of the $23.5 billion it owed in
2002. But a new accord would help calm market jitters. Mr. Simsek, who is handling negotiations, said
technical discussions would be completed in a "month or so."
On the Mediterranean coast, meanwhile, Antalya is in the middle of its busiest tourist season ever. It expects
over nine million visitors this year, up from 7.3 million last year and roughly half that in 2002. It's biggest worry
is not Islam, but forest fires that raged this week through coastal regions.
Mr. Barut, the hotelier, says Turkey might get more conservative but sees no risk of it turning into anything
like Iran. That, he says, would kill the tourism industry.
Agriculture, another local mainstay, is also growing. Antalya now exports around 350,000 tons of tomatoes a
year, nearly double the amount in 2002. Exports of flowers to Europe and Russia have soared, too.
A beneficiary of this is Ali Riza Akinci, who sells seeds for tomatoes, flowers and hotel lawns. He gets some
of these from Israel, a supplier that is shunned in the rest of the Muslim world -- but not here. Business, says
Mr. Akinci, should trump politics.
Not everyone is upbeat. The head of the Antalya's chamber of commerce, Kemal Ozgen, thinks the state is
now too hands-off. He wants it to do more to protect small shop owners, who are losing business to newly
opened shopping malls. Antalya, he says, had just one mall when the AK Party took power. It now has 28.
Foreign retailers have fueled much of this expansion. Too much competition, says Mr. Ozgen, is unhealthy.
All the same, Mr. Ozgen is happy with the benefits that have accrued to his own business, a window-making
factory. Thanks to a building blitz, he's more than tripled output since the AK Party came to power.
Antalya's growing integration in the world economy makes it highly vulnerable to any serious downturn on a
global level. For example, a recession in Germany, now Antalya's biggest tourist source, would hit local
businesses hard.
Antalya's AK Party mayor, Mr. Turel, can't do much about that. But he does try to nip less serious problems in
the bud -- such as reports in the Russian media of a crackdown on booze. Mr. Turel swiftly set the record
straight: Alcohol, he announced at a tourism conference, would never be restricted in Antalya on his watch.
More than two million Russians are expected to visit the resort this year.

--- Page 186 ---
Page 186 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Pragmatism, says the mayor, is the AK Party's guiding creed. "We allow praying, we allow bikinis, we allow
discos," the mayor says. "We allow everything."

Large Stock Focus: AMR Surges by 13%; Ford, GM Also Climb --- Investors Snap Up Stocks Hit

Stocks rallied, the Dow Jones Industrial Average rose more than 300 points and the Standard &Poor's 500

Owing to the jumps in oil and gasoline prices, malls have lost customers, airlines have turned in massive

of their purchase price.

While markets were understandably relieved, one money manager said the thesis hadn't changed one iota
after the Fed's statement, which was, if anything, cautionary on all fronts.
"I'd say the statement is neutral to hawkish," said Sean Simko, head of fixed-income management for SEI
Investments. "They're not boxing themselves in with their statement, but they are still acknowledging that
inflation is a concern." As for economic growth, "they're buying themselves some time to see if the 2%
fed-funds rate is going to stimulate growth."

posted a decline in quarterly earnings and revenue as traffic of gamblers and other sojourners held up better
than expected.

consumer-discretionary and other sectors.

Investing in Funds: A Monthly Analysis --- After a Tough First Half, Pros Say Where They Went Wrong
Investing in Funds: A Monthly Analysis --- After a Tough First Half, Pros Say Where They Went Wrong
By Diya Gullapalli
1,852 words
4 August 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
It's treacherous out there: Mortgage giants Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac face losses so potentially steep that
the Treasury Department has taken steps to prop them up. Household-name banks and giant Wall Street
firms are selling family jewels to shore up their capital. Home values keep falling, oil prices are punishingly
high, and consumer confidence has taken a dive. For investors, it's easy to go wrong -- even if you are a
veteran fund manager with one of the best long-term records around.
The average U.S. diversified stock mutual fund was down 11.7% for the first seven months of the year, the
worst showing for that period since the tough bear market of 2002, according to preliminary Lipper Inc.
figures. To get a sense of just how tough and unpredictable the markets have been, we asked fund tracker
Morningstar Inc. to search its database for managers whose returns put them in the top 25% of their
categories for the past three years, through June 30, but in the bottom 25% for the first half of 2008.
Morningstar found 62 managers. Most invest in U.S. stocks, but some shop globally and some buy bonds.
We focused on funds bigger than $500 million.
We selected five of the managers to talk about what went wrong this year. Many cited the same missteps:
They misjudged the energy boom's staying power, and they underestimated the depth of woes in the financial
sector. Looking forward, there's little agreement on what's ahead.
For individual investors who expect to be in the stock market for years, these managers' assessments are a
stark reminder of the need for a well-thought-out, diversified asset-allocation plan to ride out the rough times.
While a few of our managers have improved their standings a bit in the past month, deftly timing movements
in and out of sectors is a huge challenge even for those who've been at it for years. Here are excerpts of their
comments from our conversations:
WILLIAM FRIES, co-manager
Thornburg International Value $17.8 billion
Foreign large blend stocks
-- Three-year annualized return: 17.4%, in sixth percentile
-- First-half return: negative 13%, in 88th percentile
Certainly the most surprising aspect has been the litany of exposures that financial institutions have had. It's
been water torture in terms of how this has developed.
[Swiss bank] UBS was one we sold late in the game. We held on to that mostly because we thought the
[company's] wealth-management franchise would keep the [stock] valuation from declining to the extent that it
ultimately did. We didn't sell that until May. UBS was very painful. Call it an absence on our part of the
perception of magnitude of the problems.
We're still not completely free of the potential of write-downs in financial securities. The lesson of the first half
is that you want to ask a lot of questions, you want to try to cut through the vagaries as deeply as possible. In
the second half, we'll be faced with a slowing economy and inflationary pressures, and every stock that you
want to add to the portfolio, you have to take that into consideration.
CORY GILCHRIST, manager
Marsico 21st Century $2.3 billion
U.S. large growth stocks

--- Page 190 ---
Page 190 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
-- Three-year annualized return: 10.4%, in eighth percentile
-- First-half return: negative 16.7%, in 96th percentile
At the end of last year, we were looking for the global economy to slow. We were coming out of
unprecedented, synchronized global growth. We began to position away from companies purely benefiting
from that super-normal rate of growth. So we skewed the portfolio away from pure commodity,
price-escalation companies into more unit-growth companies like [Danish wind-turbine maker] Vestas Wind
Systems and [engineering and construction firm] Shaw Group.
Shying away from those price escalations in commodities hurt a lot [in the second quarter]. The financials and
the gaming stocks [also] did poorly in the second quarter, but recently companies like Wells Fargo and Wynn
Resorts posted strong earnings. I'm taking away that being exposed to these spaces early can backfire, with
gaming and financials as the specific examples.
We've also turned the portfolio toward the highest-quality businesses. Those are the companies we stuck with
and are still as confident in, like a Goldman Sachs or a Wynn Resorts.
There's been a lot of conflicting macroeconomic data. A lot of fear in the markets. A lack of confidence in
global financial systems. All of those things are coming to a peak right now. There are factors that took longer
to [work through] than we anticipated.
If you get commodities to flatline from here, you'll have very little inflation at all. I think that's important for the
world right now.
We evaluate each individual position all the time. I will change course if I see the data and say, "Cory, you're
just wrong." So far, we aren't at that point yet. That's why I say the next couple of months are really important.
DAN FUSS, co-manager
Loomis Sayles Strategic Income $12.6 billion
Multisector bonds
-- Three-year annualized return: 6.5%, in ninth percentile
-- First-half return: negative 2%, in 79th percentile
The thing that really stands out that's both a challenge and an opportunity is the lack of liquidity in many parts
of the bond market. On the bad-news side, it does tend to move bond prices down, not so much in Treasurys,
because the liquidity there is OK. The [overall bond] market is thin, and will remain thin. My sense of things is
that leverage in the financial markets is being reduced, but that leads to an imbalance in supply and demand.
We've been a steady buyer off and on [this year] of individual items in the corporate-bond market, mostly
investment-grade. Long-term investment-grade corporates were the poorest-performing area by far. Long
Treasurys for the year to date didn't pan out that well either.
During the second quarter, we did something we'd not done ever, or at least in the past 10 years: We actually
bought some asset-backed securities of varying types. We've been buying preferred [securities] of banks, but
they're tricky. Lehman Brothers -- it's known we've been a buyer of that and continue to be. [The fund also
has been buying Fannie Mae preferred stock recently.] You have to know your specific issues as well as your
issuers.
The preferreds we've bought are a mixed bag. You won't know if this was a good move, quite frankly, for a
number of years. That keeps a lot of people away from a market like this, and it should.
We've been buying bonds that we'll be happy about five years from now, in an environment where [interest]
rates are going up, which means we have to be buying them cheap today.
JOHN CALAMOS, co-manager
Calamos Global Growth and Income $1.3 billion
World stocks and bonds
-- Three-year annualized return: 10.6%, in 24th percentile
-- First-half return: negative 12.7%, in 95th percentile

--- Page 191 ---
Page 191 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Obviously, I think we're surprised like everyone else that oil prices continue to be a one-trick pony. Basically,
we didn't predict a $150 barrel of oil.
In financials, the big surprise has been the lack of transparency and the confusion within the
investment-banking industry, and how very smart people can't even understand exactly what is going on
there.
We're picky on which financials we're choosing. We're using convertible preferred shares instead of the
[common] stock, and limiting that exposure in the portfolio. Probably some of [our] worst performers have
been in the financials, where we have added value relative to our benchmarks by being underweight.
[For now,] the market is not valuing growth-oriented companies because of the fear of a recession.
We think as this market turns -- and our sense is we're groping for the bottom in here -- growth types of
companies will lead us out of here.
If we're entering a long-term bear market, here's what you do: Buy a case of tuna, buy a bar of gold, and
head for the hills. We don't think that's the case. We have a lot of problems, but globalization is a tremendous
force in the world. We're creating three billion people in the middle class. This is not all going to collapse
around us.
TOM O'HALLORAN, manager
Lord Abbett Developing Growth $1 billion
Small growth stocks
-- Three-year annualized return: 12.7%, in third percentile
-- First-half return: negative 16.6%, in 84th percentile
We may be bottoming. I do feel that we'll be higher at the end of the year than we are now. I don't think that
the slowdown in the economy will be as pronounced or as deep or as long as people fear. I think we're well
along in the financial crisis, and I think that energy prices are going to come down.
The continued rise in oil prices was clearly the biggest surprise.
In the last year, we went from a market that was willing to pay for the bright promise of a lot of the companies
that we own to a market that evidenced great skepticism regarding the prospects of these companies. The
big mistake was the failure to take down the size of these companies that did so well last year and not being
overweight energy [relative to other funds]. We've learned that the next time we see these signs [of sentiment
changing], we would act much more quickly to rebalance the portfolio than we did in January.
We sold out of banks 15 months ago. We concluded in the first half of the year that the selling had been
overdone in [some] names. We now have a small position in PrivateBancorp. Our biggest position [in banks]
is SVB Financial Group, which has a very secure source of deposits and very little real-estate exposure.
Although the U.S. consumer has slowed a lot, there are still many companies in emerging nations that are
participating in this global infrastructure build or the growing wealth of consumers.
In the U.S., there are many emerging consumer icons that are down, and we're buying them now. Those
would include Dick's Sporting Goods, Fossil, J.Crew, Under Armour, and Chipotle Mexican Grill.
I would say the disparity between energy performance and the rest of market this year is as extreme as tech
in 1999, and I don't think it's sustainable in the near term. So we're now underweight energy.
---
Ms. Gullapalli is a staff reporter in New York for The Wall Street Journal. She can be reached at
diya.gullapalli@wsj.com.
